Determining Genus in Riba: The Hanafi Criterion
Clarifies that different genera in riba transactions allow unequal exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion (origin, name, purpose) to determine whether two items are the same genus for riba purposes, and state the ruling on exchanging them in unequal amounts.

Core Idea
Different genera allow unequal exchange; unity of genus is proven by zakat joining rules.

The permissibility of exchanging one commodity for another in unequal amounts depends on whether they belong to the same genus (jins). The Hanafi school defines genus using specific criteria—origin, name, and purpose—and uses a unique analogy from zakat law. This lesson unpacks those criteria using the text from Bahru al-Raiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Locate the core ruling in the passage: different meats, milks, and vinegars may be exchanged in unequal amounts. Note the condition that they must be from different genera.
The ruling is permissive (mubah) when genera differ.
2
Extract the Criteria for Genus
The passage mentions three criteria: origin (asl), name (ism), and purpose (maqsud). For example, barley flour and wheat flour have different origins and names, thus different genera.
These criteria limit the concept of genus; otherwise all food would be one genus.
3
Analyze the Proof: Damm fi al-Zakah
Understand the proof: the Hanafi school uses the rule of joining different livestock to complete zakat nisab as evidence. If two animals are not joined in zakat, they are considered different genera for riba.
This qiyas is unique to Hanafi; other schools may not accept it.
4
Apply to Examples
Apply the criteria and proof to the examples given: beef and buffalo are same genus because they are joined in zakat; beef and mutton are different. Similarly, goat hair and sheep wool differ in purpose.
Processing (sina'ah) can also change genus, e.g., cooked vs. raw oil.
5
Note Exceptions and Edge Cases
Even within same genus, if parts have different purposes or undergo transformation, they become different genera. This prevents overly restrictive rulings.
The criterion is specific meaning (al-ma'na al-khass), not general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللحوم المختلفة بعضها ببعض متفاضلاً ولبن البقر والغنم وخل الدقل بخل العنب لأن أصولها أجناس مختلفة يضم بعضها إلى بعض في الزكاة، وأسماؤها أيضاً مختلفة باعتبار الإضافة كدقيق الشعير والبر والمقصود أيضاً يختلف، والمعتبر في الاتحاد المعنى الخاص دون العام، ولو اعتبر العام لما جاز بيع شيء بشيء أصلاً قيد بالمختلفة لأن غيرها لا يجوز متفاضلاً كلحم البقر والجاموس أو لبنهما أو لحم المعز والضأن أو لبنها أو لحم العراب والبخاتي لاتحاد الجنس بدليل الضم في الزكاة للتكميل فكذا أجزاؤهما ما لم يختلف المقصود كشعر المعز وصوف الضأن أو ما يتبدل بالصنعة لاختلاف المقاصد.
And various meats one with another in unequal amounts, and cow’s milk and sheep’s milk, and vinegar of inferior dates with grape vinegar, because their origins are different genera, some are joined to others in zakat, and their names are also different by attribution like barley flour and wheat, and the purpose also differs, and what is considered in unification is the specific meaning not the general, and if the general were considered, no sale of one thing for another would be permissible at all. He restricted it to different, because other than them is not permissible in unequal amounts, like beef and buffalo meat or their milk, or goat and sheep meat or their milk, or Arabian camel and Bactrian camel meat due to unity of genus evidenced by joining in zakat for completion, so likewise their parts unless the purpose differs, like goat hair and sheep wool, or what changes by processing due to differing purposes.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi criterion for determining genus (jins) in riba transactions. The key factors are origin (asl), name (ism), and purpose (maqsud). Items of different genera may be exchanged in unequal amounts. For example, beef and buffalo meat are the same genus because they are both cattle and are joined in zakat for completing nisab, so they cannot be exchanged in unequal amounts. But beef and mutton are different genera, so unequal exchange is permissible. Similarly, milk from cows and sheep are different genera. Even within the same animal, parts with different purposes (e.g., goat hair vs. sheep wool) are considered different genera. Processing (sina'ah) also changes the genus, as with cooked vs. raw oil. The ruling relies on the principle of joining in zakat (damm fi al-zakah) as evidence for determining genus identity.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Genus or species; in riba, items of the same jins require equality in quantity when exchanged.
ضم في الزكاة (damm fi al-zakah): Joining different types of livestock to complete the zakat nisab; used as evidence for genus identity.
المعنى الخاص (al-ma'na al-khass): The specific meaning or attribute; used to determine genus rather than a general category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following pairs cannot be exchanged in unequal amounts?
A) Beef and lamb
B) Cow's milk and goat's milk
C) Beef and buffalo meat
D) Date vinegar and grape vinegar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines genus in riba by origin, name, and purpose, and uses the rule of joining in zakat to identify same genus, allowing unequal exchange between different genera.

Ruling on Selling Bread with Wheat or Flour in Excess
Ruling on exchanging bread for grain in unequal amounts, with proof from processing and intra-madhhab dispute on salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging bread for grain or flour in unequal amounts and explain why processing changes the genus for riba purposes.

Core Idea
Processing changes the genus: bread is a different commodity from wheat, so unequal exchange is permitted.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common market transaction: exchanging bread for its raw ingredients. The key Hanafi insight is that processing (sina'ah) transforms a commodity into a different genus, removing the riba condition of equality. We will see how this applies to spot sales and salam contracts, and the intra-madhhab difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the ruling on exchanging bread for wheat or flour in unequal amounts in a spot sale?
The passage states it is permissible.
2
Locate the proof
Why does processing (sina'ah) change the genus? How does it affect the categories of makil and mawzun?
Bread becomes a different genus and is no longer measured by volume.
3
Analyze the salam dispute
What is the ruling when bread is the deferred item? Note the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
Abu Hanifa invalidates; Sahibayn allow by analogy with meat.
4
Understand the tarjih
Why is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad relied upon in fatwa?
Due to common practice and analogy with meat salam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخبز بالبر أو بالدقيق متفاضلا لأن الخبز بالصنعة صار جنساً آخر حتى يخرج من أن يكون مكيلاً والبر والدقيق مكيلان فلم يجمعهما القدر ولا الجنس حتى جاز بيع أحدهما بالآخر نسيئة إذا كانت الحنطة هي المتأخرة لإمكان ضبطها. وإن كان الخبز هو المتأخر فالسلم فيه لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة لأنه يتفاوت بالطحن والعجن والنضج . واختلف على قولهما فمنهم من جوزه على قياس المسلم باللحم وبه يفتى للتعامل
And bread with wheat or flour in unequal amounts, because bread by processing became another genus so that it exits from being measured (makil), and wheat and flour are measured, so neither measure nor genus unites them, thus it is permissible to sell one for the other on credit if the wheat is the deferred item because it can be specified. But if the bread is the deferred, then salam therein is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa because it varies by grinding, kneading, and cooking. And there is difference on their view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), some permit it by analogy with selling meat, and with this fatwa is given due to transactions.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of selling bread in exchange for wheat or flour in unequal amounts. Because bread has undergone processing (sina'ah) - grinding, kneading, baking - it becomes a different genus from raw wheat or flour. Additionally, bread is no longer measured by volume (kayl) but by weight or count, so the requirement of equality is removed. Hence, it is permissible to sell bread for wheat in unequal amounts. However, if the sale is on credit (salam) and bread is the deferred item, Abu Hanifa rules it invalid due to variability in the product. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it by analogy with meat salam, and this is the relied-upon fatwa due to common practice.

Key Terms
صنعة (sina'ah): Processing or manufacturing; changes the genus of a commodity in riba rulings.
مكيل (makil): Something measured by volume (kayl); bread is not makil due to processing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa, is salam (forward buying) of bread permissible?
A) No, because bread cannot be specified.
B) Yes, by analogy with meat salam.
C) Yes, but only if the bread is weighed.
D) No, because it involves riba.

Key Takeaway
Processing (sina'ah) changes the genus of a commodity for riba purposes, allowing unequal exchange of bread with grain, and for salam, the relied-upon view permits it by analogy with meat.

Prohibition of Selling Wheat with Flour or Sawiq and Ruling on Flour with Flour
Forbids unequal or equal exchange of wheat with its derivatives; allows flour-for-flour only equally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging wheat with its derivatives (flour and sawiq) and apply the conditions for permissible flour-for-flour exchange.

Core Idea
Wheat and its derivatives are the same genus; exchange is prohibited unless equal in measure, with special rules for pressed flour.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the prohibition of selling wheat for flour or sawiq due to riba suspicion. It highlights the importance of genus and measurement standard (kayl vs. weight) in commodity exchanges. The passage also clarifies the permissibility of flour-for-flour exchange when equal, and introduces Ibn al-Fadl's nuance regarding pressed flour.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited transactions
The passage prohibits selling wheat for flour or sawiq, whether equal or unequal. Why? They are same genus despite different names.
Note the contrast with sesame oil/seeds where weight standard permits inequality.
2
Understand the reason (illah)
The prohibition is due to riba suspicion because the standard is volume (kayl) and the derivative introduces uncertainty.
3
Analyze flour-for-flour ruling
Flour for flour is allowed only if equal in measure because of identical genus, form, and meaning. No consideration of potential inequality.
Analogous to wheat-for-wheat.
4
Examine Ibn al-Fadl's restriction
If flour is pressed, volume measure may not accurately reflect equality; then weight is considered, with two narrations.
The relied-upon view: permissibility by weight if equal.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Equal measure for unpressed flour; for pressed, permissibility by weight if equal is the stronger view.
This is the operative fatwa position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بيع البر بالدقيق أو بالسويق أي لا يجوز بيع الحنطة بأحدهما متفاضلاً ولا متساوياً لأنه جنس من وجه، وإن خص باسم آخر فيحرم لشبهة الربا والمعيار فيهما الكيل وهو غير مسو لهما بيع دهن السمسم بالسمسم حيث يجوز لأن المعيار فيه الوزن وهو مسو، والسويق ما يجرش من الشعير والحنطة وغيرهما. وقيد بالبر الخ أي لأن بيع الدقيق بالدقيق متساوياً ولا يجوز متفاضلاً لاتحاد الجنس والصورة والمعنى، ولا عبرة باحتمال التفاضل كما في البر بالبر. وقيده ابن الفضل بما إذا كانا مكبوسين وإلا لا يجوز، وإن باعه بمثله موازنة ففيه روايتان
There is no sale of wheat with flour or sawiq, i.e., it is not permissible to sell wheat with either of them in unequal or equal amounts, because they are of the same genus in one sense, and although named differently, it is forbidden due to suspicion of riba. The standard for both is measure (kayl), which does not permit them, unlike selling sesame oil with sesame seeds which is permissible because the standard is weight, which permits it. Sawiq is what is ground from barley, wheat, etc. He restricted with wheat etc. because selling flour with flour is permissible in equal amounts and not permissible in unequal amounts due to unity of genus, form, and meaning, and no consideration is given to potential inequality as in wheat with wheat. Ibn al-Fadl restricted it to when they are pressed (makbusayn), otherwise not permissible. And if one sells it by weight equal, there are two narrations.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage states the prohibition of selling wheat (حنطة) in exchange for its direct derivatives, flour (دقيق) or sawiq (سويق, parched grain), whether in equal or unequal amounts. The reason is they are considered the same genus (jins) for riba purposes, despite different names. The standard for both is volume measure (kayl), which prohibits unequal exchange, and even equal exchange is forbidden because of potential variation in processing (similar to selling wheat for wheat in equal measure is allowed, but here the derivative introduces uncertainty). In contrast, selling sesame oil for sesame seeds is allowed in unequal amounts because the standard is weight, not volume. Sawiq is defined as ground barley, wheat, or other grains. The passage then discusses the ruling on flour-for-flour exchange: it is permissible only if equal in measure, because they are identical in genus, form, and purpose. However, if the flour is pressed (compressed, مكبوس) such that volume differs, it may not be permissible. There are two narrations on selling flour by weight (weighing equal amounts).

Key Terms
سويق (sawiq): Parched grain (barley, wheat, etc.) ground into a powder; considered same genus as the grain.
مكبوس (makbus): Pressed or compacted flour/similar; affects volume measure, so equality may not hold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is it permissible to sell one kilogram of wheat for one kilogram of flour?
A) Yes, because they are both measured by weight.
B) No, because they are the same genus and the standard is volume, not weight.
C) Yes, because they are different products.
D) No, because flour is not a commodity.

Key Takeaway
Wheat and its derivatives (flour, sawiq) are the same genus; exchange is prohibited due to riba suspicion unless identical items and equal in measure, with pressed flour exceptions.

The Three Causes of Differentiation in Riba Exchanges
Explains rules for unequal exchange of certain foodstuffs and the principles differentiating them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi factors that permit unequal exchange (tafadul) in riba and apply them to identify permissible transactions.

Core Idea
Three factors—difference in origin, purpose, or craftsmanship—allow unequal exchange even between similar food items.

In the Hanafi school, riba (usury) prohibits unequal exchange of two items of the same type measured by weight or capacity. However, this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that certain items may be exchanged with disparity because they differ in origin, purpose, or have undergone manufacturing. Understanding these three factors is crucial for correctly identifying riba-free transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Start with the general rule: unequal exchange of two items of the same type and same measurement standard (weight/capacity) is prohibited riba. Then note the exceptions.
The passage lists several exceptions: bird meat, abdominal fat, bread, etc.
2
Analyze the first exception: bird meat
Bird meat is not customarily weighed, so the effective cause ('illa) of measurement is absent. Therefore, unequal exchange is allowed even within the same species, except chicken and goose in Egypt where they are weighed.
This shows the importance of custom (urf) in determining the 'illa.
3
Analyze the second exception: fat types
Abdominal fat (shahm al-batn) and tail fat (aliyah) are both from sheep but differ in name, form, and purpose. Hence they are considered different types, allowing disparity.
Difference in purpose (maqsud) is a key factor.
4
Analyze the third exception: bread vs. wheat/flour
Bread is a manufactured product; it is no longer measured by capacity like wheat or flour. Thus it belongs to a different type, allowing unequal exchange or even credit sales.
Craftsmanship (sin'a) changes the item's legal category.
5
Extract the general principle
From these cases, the Hanafi jurists derive three factors that make items different: difference in origin (asl), difference in purpose (maqsud), and change by craftsmanship (sin'a). These override the riba prohibition.
This principle applies to other items like metal vessels, unless gold/silver.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب الربا البر بالدقيق أو العنب وشحم البطن بالألية أو باللحم والخبز بالبر أو بالدقيق متفاضلا لا بيع منه متفاضلاً. وإنما جاز بيع لحم الطير بعضه ببعض متفاضلاً وإن كان من جنس واحد لم جواز كذا يتبدل بالصنعة لكونه غير موزون عادة فلم يكن مقدراً فلم توجد العلة، فحاصله أن الاختلاف باختلاف الأصل أو المقصود أو تبدل الصنعة. وفي فتح القدير : وينبغي أن يستثنى من لحوم الطير الدجاج والأوز فإنه يوزن في عادة ديار أهل مصر بعظمه. والدقل رديء التمر يجوز خل التمر بخل العنب متفاضلاً وكذا عصيره رهما لاختلاف أصلهما جنساً، وتخصيص الدقل باعتبار العادة لأن الدقل هو الذي كان يتخذ خلاً في العادة اهـ. والحاصل أن ما يوجب اختلاف الأمور ثلاثة : اختلاف الأصول واختلاف المقاصد وزيادة الصنعة ومنها إناء صفر أو حديد أحدهما أثقل من الآخر، وكذا قمقمة بقمقمتين، وإبرة بإبرتين، بيع وخودة بخودتين، وسيف بسيفين وداوة بدواتين ما لم يكن شيء من ذلك من أحد النقدين فيمتنع التفاضل وإن اصطلحوا بعد الصياغة على ترك الوزن والاقتصار على العد والصورة؛ في فتح القدير قوله : (وشحم البطن بالألية أو باللحم أي يصح بيعها متفاضلاً وإن كانت كلها من الضأن لأنها أجناس مختلفة لاختلاف الأسماء والصور والمقاصد قوله : (والخبز بالبر أو بالدقيق (متفاضلا) لأن الخبز بالصنعة صار جنساً آخر حتى يخرج من أن يكون مكيلاً والبر والدقيق مكيلان فلم يجمعهما القدر ولا الجنس حتى جاز بيع أحدهما بالآخر نسيئة إذا المتأخرة لإمكان ضبطها. وإن كان الخبز هو المتأخر فالسلم فيه لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة لأنه يتفاوت بالطحن والعجن والنضج . واختلف على قولهما فمنهم من جوزه على قياس المسلم باللحم وبه يفتى للتعامل وفي الحاوي: يجوز بيع اللبن بالجبن اهـ
Book of Sales, Chapter of Riba: Wheat with flour or grapes, abdominal fat with tail fat or meat, bread with wheat or flour — (sold) with disparity, not selling from it with disparity. And it is permitted to sell bird meat with each other with disparity even if of the same type, because it is not customarily weighed, so it is not measured, so the effective cause does not exist. So the summary is that difference is by difference of origin or purpose or change by manufacture. And in Fath al-Qadeer: It should be excepted from bird meats chicken and goose, for they are weighed in the custom of the lands of Egypt with their bones. And the basest dates: vinegar of dates with vinegar of grapes is permissible with disparity, and likewise their juice, due to difference of their origins in type, and specification of basest dates by consideration of custom because the basest dates are those that were customarily made into vinegar. End. And the conclusion is that what necessitates difference of things are three: difference of origins, difference of purposes, and increase of craftsmanship. Among them is a copper or iron vessel, one heavier than the other, and likewise a flask with two flasks, a needle with two needles, a helmet with two helmets, a sword with two swords, an inkwell with two inkwells, as long as none of that is of one of the two currencies, then disparity is prohibited even if they agree after forging to abandon weighing and restrict to counting and form. In Fath al-Qadeer: His saying: (And abdominal fat with tail fat or meat) i.e., selling them with disparity is valid even if all are from sheep, because they are different types due to difference of names, forms, and purposes. His saying: (And bread with wheat or flour with disparity) because bread, by manufacture, becomes another type such that it exits from being measured by capacity, while wheat and flour are measured by capacity, so neither capacity nor type unites them, so selling one with the other on credit is permitted because the deferred one can be controlled. But if the bread is the deferred item, then salam in it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa because it varies by grinding, kneading, and ripeness. And differing transmission from his two companions: some permit it by analogy to salam in meat, and by it fatwa is given for dealing. And in al-Hawi: selling milk with cheese is permissible. End.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key principle in the Hanafi rules of riba (usury): unequal exchange (tafadul) is prohibited if the two items share the same effective cause ('illa) of measurement (by weight or capacity) and are of the same type. However, the passage lists exceptions where inequality is allowed because the items differ in origin, purpose, or have undergone a manufacturing change. For example, bird meat is not customarily weighed, so the 'illa is absent, allowing unequal exchange even within the same species. Chicken and goose are excepted in Egypt because they are weighed. Similarly, abdominal fat (shahm al-batn) and tail fat (aliyah) are considered different types despite both being from sheep, due to different names, forms, and uses. Bread made from wheat or flour is considered a new type due to manufacturing, so unequal exchange is allowed; it is no longer measured by capacity like raw grain. The passage also discusses salam (advance payment) for bread: Abu Hanifa prohibits it because bread quality varies, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ, and later fatwa follows the permissive view for practical necessity. A general rule is stated: three factors allow items to be considered different: 1) different origins (e.g., dates vs. grapes), 2) different purposes (e.g., tail fat vs. abdominal fat), 3) increase by craftsmanship (e.g., bread from flour, utensils from raw metal). However, if the items are of silver or gold (currency), disparity remains prohibited regardless of craftsmanship.

Key Terms
الربا (al-riba): Usury or unlawful increase in exchange of certain commodities.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause for prohibition of riba: being measured by weight or capacity.
الاختلاف باختلاف الأصل أو المقصود أو تبدل الصنعة (al-ikhtilaf bi-ikhtilaf al-asl aw al-maqsud aw tabaddul al-sin'a): The three factors that make items different types: difference in origin, purpose, or change by craftsmanship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it permissible to sell bird meat of the same type with disparity (tafadul)?
A) Because all birds are considered different types.
B) Because bird meat is not customarily weighed, so the 'illa of measurement is absent.
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ specifically allowed it.
D) Because bird meat is considered a manufactured good.

Key Takeaway
Three factors—difference in origin, purpose, or craftsmanship—determine when items are considered different types, permitting unequal exchange in Hanafi riba rules.

Selling Wheat with Flour or Saweeq: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on riba al-fadl with wheat derivatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on exchanging wheat with flour or saweeq, identify the mufta bihi, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Is flour a different type from wheat? Abu Yusuf says yes; Abu Hanifa says no.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: may you exchange wheat for flour or saweeq with inequality? The core question is whether manufacturing (grinding) changes the type (jins) for riba purposes. We will explore the positions of the three major Hanafi imams and see why the fatwa ultimately adopts the permissive view of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed transaction
The transaction is exchanging wheat (bar) for its derivatives: flour (daqeeq) or saweeq (parched grain). The question is whether they are the same type (jins) for riba al-fadl, and whether exchange with disparity is allowed.
Riba al-fadl requires equality in both quantity and hand-to-hand delivery if the items are the same type.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that flour and saweeq are essentially the same type as wheat because they are parts of the grain. Grinding does not create a new type; the increase in volume is only apparent. Therefore, any exchange with inequality is haram (riba al-fadl).
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his reasoning
Abu Yusuf considers flour and saweeq as different types from wheat because manufacturing changes the commodity. He permits unequal exchange but only hand-to-hand (to avoid nasi'ah). His evidence: bread is considered different from flour, similarly grinding changes wheat.
Abu Yusuf's view is transmitted as a weak riwayah but becomes the mufta bihi.
4
State Muhammad al-Shaybani's position
Muhammad generally agrees with Abu Hanifa (prohibition), but there is also a transmission from him permitting the exchange like Abu Yusuf. He seems to have two views.
The passage notes that Muhammad's clear position is prohibition, but another riwayah shows permission.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
Al-Hidayah and later fatwa authorities adopt Abu Yusuf's permissive view for practical ease. The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, one cannot take more flour than wheat given; under Abu Yusuf, one can, as long as hand-to-hand.
The fatwa is on ease, but the principle remains contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف على قولهما) عبارة الهداية : وإن كان الخبر نسيئة يجوز عند أبي يوسف وعليه الفتوى. وفي فتح القدير : لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة وكذا عند محمد، ويجوز عند أبي يوسف. وذكر الزيلعي ما هنا عن النهاية معزياً إلى المبسوط وما في الهداية والفتح عن الكافي عن ابن رستم فالظاهر أن عن أبي يوسف روايتين تأمل قوله : (وهو غير مسولهما قال الزيلعي : ألا ترى أن البر إذا طحن يريد عليه وتلك الزيادة كانت موجودة في الحال وظهرت بالطحن قوله : (وقيد بالبر الخ) أي لأن بيع الدقيق بالسويق والزيتون بالزيت والسمسم بالشيرج حتى يكون الزيت والشيرج أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم يجوز إلا متساوياً كما في الخلاصة. وقيد بالبر لأن بيع الدقيق بالسويق لا يجوز مطلقاً عنده، وجاز عندهما مطلقاً لاختلاف الجنس ولكن يداً بيد لأن القدر يجمعهما. وله أنهما جنس واحد من وجه لأنهما من أجزاء الحنطة، وبيع المقلية بالمقلية والسويق بالسويق متساوياً جائز لاتحاد الاسم.
(And differing on the statement of his two companions) The expression of al-Hidayah: If the bread is on credit, it is permitted according to Abu Yusuf, and upon it is the fatwa. And in Fath al-Qadeer: It is not permitted according to Abu Hanifa, and likewise according to Muhammad, and it is permitted according to Abu Yusuf. And al-Zayla'i mentioned what is here from al-Nihayah attributed to al-Mabsut, and what is in al-Hidayah and al-Fath from al-Kafi from Ibn Rustam, so it appears that from Abu Yusuf there are two transmissions. Reflect on his statement: (And it is not permissible for both) al-Zayla'i said: Do you not see that when wheat is ground, it increases? And that increase was present immediately and appeared by grinding. His statement: (And he restricted by wheat, etc.) i.e., because selling flour with saweeq and olives with oil and sesame with shiraj such that the oil and shiraj are more than what is in the olives and sesame is permissible except equally as in al-Khulasah. And he restricted by wheat because selling flour with saweeq is not permissible at all according to him (Abu Hanifa), and it is permissible according to his two companions absolutely due to difference of type, but hand-to-hand, because capacity unites them. And for him (Abu Hanifa) they are one type in one respect because they are parts of wheat, and selling fried grain with fried grain and saweeq with saweeq equally is permissible due to unity of name.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on riba, focusing on the ruling of selling wheat (bar) with its derivatives: flour (daqeeq) and saweeq (parched grain). The Hanafi school has significant internal differences. Abu Hanifa holds that flour and saweeq are essentially the same type as wheat because they are parts of the grain, so any exchange with inequality is prohibited. However, Abu Yusuf considers them different types, allowing unequal exchange but only hand-to-hand (to avoid deferred riba). Muhammad al-Shaybani aligns with Abu Hanifa in some transmissions but is reported to also have a permissive view. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) according to al-Hidayah and later scholars is the permissive view of Abu Yusuf for practical ease. The passage also clarifies other cases: selling flour with saweeq is prohibited absolutely according to Abu Hanifa, but allowed by his two companions; selling olives with oil or sesame with shiraj (tahini) is allowed only when the extracted product is more than the amount in the original, to avoid riba al-fadl. The key principle is that items from the same origin that undergo manufacturing change may be considered different types, but this is contested. The text also notes that selling items like fried grain (muqliya) with itself or saweeq with itself is allowed equally due to name unity.

Key Terms
السويق (al-saweeq): Parched grain, usually made from wheat or barley by grinding coarsely.
النسيئة (al-nasi'ah): Credit sale; here referring to deferred payment in exchange for bread.
الدقيق (al-daqeeq): Flour, specifically wheat flour.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school regarding selling wheat with flour with disparity, which imam's view is followed?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Zufar

Key Takeaway
Though Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibit unequal exchange of wheat with flour, the mufta bihi adopts Abu Yusuf's permissive view for practical ease, allowing disparity as long as the exchange is hand-to-hand.

Exchanging Olives for Oil: Conditions to Avoid Riba
Ruling on exchanging oil-bearing items with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where exchanging olives for oil is invalid and apply the condition that the separated oil must exceed the oil content.

Core Idea
Exchanging oil-bearing items for their extract is valid only if the extract exceeds the oil inside, ensuring the pulp has compensation.

When olives are exchanged for olive oil, the presence of both oil and pulp (tafl) creates a potential for riba al-fadl. The Hanafi school specifies strict conditions to prevent any unrequited excess. This lesson examines the rules for such exchanges and similar commodities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the specific transaction: exchanging olives for oil. The passage states it is invalid in three scenarios.
The central ruling: the sale is faasid (invalid) unless the separated oil is known to be more than the oil in the olives.
2
Analyze the three invalid cases
List each case: (1) oil in olives is more, (2) equal, (3) unknown. Understand why each leads to riba: the pulp (with value) is left uncompensated.
The pulp's value is the key factor.
3
Recognize the exception: Zufar's view
Note that Zufar within the Hanafi school allows the sale even when amounts are unknown, based on the principle of permissibility.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Extend the ruling to similar cases
Apply the same logic to walnuts with oil, milk with butter, dates with pits, and any item where the byproduct has value.
If the byproduct has no value (e.g., gold dust), the condition does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والزيتون بالزيت والسمسم بالشيرج حتى يكون الزيت والشيرج أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم أي لا يجوز البيع في ثلاث صور الأولى أن يعلم أن الزيت الذي في الزيتون أكثر لتحقق الفضل من الدهن والتفل. الثانية أن يعلم التساوي لخلو التفل عن العوض الثالثة أن لا يعلم أنه مثل أو أكثر أو أقل فلا يصح عندنا لأن الفضل المتوهم كالمتحقق احتياطاً، وعند زفر جاز لأن الجواز هو الأصل والفساد لوجود الفضل الخالي فما لم لا يفسد، ويجوز البيع في صرة بالإجماع أن يعلم أن الزيت المنفصل أكثر ليكون الفضل بالتفل، وكذا بيع الجوز بدهنه واللبن بسمنه والتمر بنواه وكل شيء لتفله قيمة إذا بيع بالخالص منه لا يجوز حتى يكون الخالص أكثر وإن لم يكن لتفله قيمة كتراب الذهب إذا بيع بالذهب أو تراب الفضة إذا بيع بالفضة لا يشترط أن يكون الذهب أو الفضة أكثر مما في التراب لأن التراب لا قيمة له فلا يجعل بإزائه شيء حتى لو جعل فسد لربا الفضل. وفي الحاوي : وإن باع حنطة بحنطة في سنبلها لم يجز وإن باع قصيل حنطة بحنطة كيلاً وجزافاً جاز وإن لم يشترط الترك اهـ قوله: (ويستقرض الخبز وزنا لا عدداً) وهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد يستقرض بهما، وعند أبي حنيفة لا يستقرض بهما، وذكر الشارح أن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف. وفي شرح المجمع: الفتوى على قول محمد. وفي فتح القدير : وأنا أرى أن قول محمد أحسن. وفي الجوهرة قال محمد ثلاث من الدناءة استقراض الخبز والجلوس على باب الحمام والنظر في مرآة الحجام .اهـ. وفي المجتبى باع رغيفاً نقداً برغيفين نسيئة يجوز، ولو كان الرغيفان نقداً والرغيف نسيئة لا يجوز، ولو باع كسيرات الخبز يجوز نقداً ونسيئة كيف كان قوله : (ولا) ربا بين المولى وعبده) لأنه وما في يده ملكه . أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه وأما إذا كان مستغرقاً فيجري الربا بينهما اتفاقاً لعدم الملك عنده للمولى في كسبه كالمكاتب وعندهما لتعلق حق الغير، والتحقيق أنه
His saying: (And olives with oil and sesame with shiraj such that the oil and shiraj are more than what is in the olives and sesame) i.e., the sale is not valid in three cases: first, if it is known that the oil in the olives is more, because of the realized excess of the oil and the pulp; second, if equality is known, because the pulp is without compensation; third, if it is not known whether it is equal, more, or less, then it is not valid according to us (Hanafis) because the suspected excess is like realized out of caution. According to Zufar, it is valid because permissibility is the principle, and invalidity is due to the existence of unrequited excess, so as long as it is not proven, it is not invalid. And the sale is permitted unanimously in the case where it is known that the separate oil is more, so that the excess is by the pulp. And likewise, the sale of walnuts with their oil, milk with its butter, dates with their pits, and everything that has pulp of value, when sold with the pure extract, is not permissible unless the pure extract is more. And if the pulp has no value, like gold dust when sold with gold or silver dust when sold with silver, it is not stipulated that the gold or silver be more than what is in the dust, because the dust has no value, so nothing is placed against it; if it is placed, it is invalid due to riba al-fadl. And in al-Hawi: If one sells wheat with wheat in its ears, it is not permissible; but if one sells a bundle of wheat with wheat by measure or by estimation, it is permissible even if the condition of leaving is not stipulated. End. His saying: (And one borrows bread by weight, not by count) This is according to Abu Yusuf; according to Muhammad, one borrows by both; according to Abu Hanifa, one borrows by neither. And the commentator mentioned that the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf. And in Sharh al-Majma': the fatwa is on the saying of Muhammad. And in Fath al-Qadeer: And I see that the saying of Muhammad is better. And in al-Jawharah, Muhammad said: Three things are of lowliness: borrowing bread, sitting at the door of the bathhouse, and looking into the barber's mirror. End. And in al-Mujtaba: If one sells a loaf for cash in exchange for two loaves on credit, it is permissible; if the two loaves are cash and the one loaf on credit, it is not permissible; and if one sells pieces of bread, it is permissible for cash and on credit however it may be. His saying: (And there is no riba between a master and his slave) because he and what is in his hand are his property. This is absolute, and it is restricted when there is no debt consuming his value and earnings. But if it is consuming, then riba runs between them by agreement, due to the lack of ownership of the master over his earnings, like the mukatab; and according to his two companions, due to the attachment of the right of others. And the verification is...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage addresses several specific issues within the chapter of riba. First, the rule of exchanging olives for oil (or sesame for shiraj/tahini). Since olives contain both oil and pulp (tafl), the exchange is only valid if the separated oil exceeds the oil content of the olives, so that the pulp compensates for the excess. If the oil is less or equal, or if the amounts are unknown, the sale is invalid to avoid riba. Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, differs and allows it based on the principle of permissibility. The same rule applies to walnuts with oil, milk with butter, dates with pits, etc. However, if the pulp has no value (like dust from gold or silver), the condition does not apply. Second, the issue of borrowing bread (qard) can be done by weight or count according to different imams: Abu Yusuf allows by weight only, Muhammad allows both, and Abu Hanifa disallows entirely. The later fatwa varies: al-Hidayah follows Abu Yusuf, but Fath al-Qadeer prefers Muhammad's view. The passage also records a saying of Muhammad that borrowing bread is a lowly act. In the sale of bread, specific rules apply: selling one loaf now for two later is valid (the deferred amount is considered a salam), but the reverse is invalid because the immediate delivery of less and deferral of more creates riba. Selling pieces of bread is always permissible. Third, there is no riba between a master and his slave because the slave's property belongs to the master, so it is like one person dealing with himself. However, if the slave has a debt that consumes his value and earnings, then riba applies because the master's ownership is incomplete. This is the position according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
التفل (al-tifl): The pulp or residue left after extracting oil; considered a valuable byproduct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Valid because of equality
B) Invalid because the pulp is without compensation
C) Valid if sold by weight
D) Valid if sold hand-to-hand

Key Takeaway
Exchanging items with a valuable byproduct for their pure extract is valid only when the extract exceeds the content in the original, ensuring the byproduct is compensated.

Borrowing Bread: Intra-Hanafi Differences on Weight vs. Count
Ruling on borrowing bread with three conflicting opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on borrowing bread (qard) and apply the fatwa according to the preferred view.

Core Idea
Borrowing bread is permitted by weight or count according to Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf only allows by weight and Abu Hanifa forbids it entirely.

The permissibility of borrowing bread (istiqrad) is a point of debate within the Hanafi school. While some consider it a lowly act, the legal discussion focuses on whether the loan must be by weight or can also be by count. This lesson unpacks the three opinions and the eventual fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the three positions
List the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding borrowing bread: AH prohibits entirely, AY allows only by weight, M allows both methods.
Note the reason for difference: bread is both similar and dissimilar in weight and count.
2
Understand the basis for each view
Abu Hanifa: prohibits because bread is a foodstuff and must be exchanged measure for measure; since loaves vary, it leads to riba. Abu Yusuf: allows by weight because weight is precise and avoids uncertainty. Muhammad: allows both because count also ensures equivalence if loaves are similar.
The principle of riba in foodstuffs (riba al-fadl) is the background.
3
Identify the mufta bihi view
Different later scholars gave fatwa on different views. The passage records that Fath al-Qadeer prefers Muhammad's view as better.
The mufta bihi is not unanimous; but Fath al-Qadeer's preference is influential.
4
Apply the ruling to a transaction
Given the fatwa on Muhammad's view, borrowing bread by weight or count is permissible, though the better practice is to borrow by weight to avoid uncertainty.

No Riba Between Master and Slave: Ownership and Exceptions
Ruling on riba in master-slave transactions with an exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rule that no riba occurs between a master and his slave, and state the exception when the slave has a debt consuming his value.

Core Idea
No riba between master and slave because the slave's property belongs to the master; exception when the slave is debt-ridden and his earnings belong to creditors.

In a master-slave relationship, since the slave and his wealth are owned by the master, transactions between them are like one person dealing with himself, and thus riba cannot occur. However, if the slave has a debt that consumes his entire value and earnings, the master's ownership is restricted, and riba can occur. This lesson explores this rule and its nuances.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The passage states: 'And there is no riba between a master and his slave because he and what is in his hand are his property.'
This is the default position.
2
Identify the restriction
The rule is not absolute. It is restricted to the case where the slave has no debt that consumes his value and earnings (ghair mustaghriq).
If the slave has a debt equal to or greater than his value, the master's ownership is limited.
3
Understand the exception case
When the slave's debt is 'mustaghriq' (consuming his value and earnings), riba does run between master and slave by agreement of the school, because the master no longer fully owns the slave's earnings.
This applies to the mukatab as well.
4
Note the difference between imams
The passage mentions 'according to his two companions' regarding the attachment of the rights of others; this refers to the reasoning of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The verification continues from the text.
The full verification is cut off in the passage.

قوله : (والزيتون بالزيت والسمسم بالشيرج حتى يكون الزيت والشيرج أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم أي لا يجوز البيع في ثلاث صور الأولى أن يعلم أن الزيت الذي في الزيتون أكثر لتحقق الفضل من الدهن والتفل. الثانية أن يعلم التساوي لخلو التفل عن العوض الثالثة أن لا يعلم أنه مثل أو أكثر أو أقل فلا يصح عندنا لأن الفضل المتوهم كالمتحقق احتياطاً، وعند زفر جاز لأن الجواز هو الأصل والفساد لوجود الفضل الخالي فما لم لا يفسد، ويجوز البيع في صرة بالإجماع أن يعلم أن الزيت المنفصل أكثر ليكون الفضل بالتفل، وكذا بيع الجوز بدهنه واللبن بسمنه والتمر بنواه وكل شيء لتفله قيمة إذا بيع بالخالص منه لا يجوز حتى يكون الخالص أكثر وإن لم يكن لتفله قيمة كتراب الذهب إذا بيع بالذهب أو تراب الفضة إذا بيع بالفضة لا يشترط أن يكون الذهب أو الفضة أكثر مما في التراب لأن التراب لا قيمة له فلا يجعل بإزائه شيء حتى لو جعل فسد لربا الفضل. وفي الحاوي : وإن باع حنطة بحنطة في سنبلها لم يجز وإن باع قصيل حنطة بحنطة كيلاً وجزافاً جاز وإن لم يشترط الترك اهـ قوله: (ويستقرض الخبز وزنا لا عدداً) وهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد يستقرض بهما، وعند أبي حنيفة لا يستقرض بهما، وذكر الشارح أن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف. وفي شرح المجمع: الفتوى على قول محمد. وفي فتح القدير : وأنا أرى أن قول محمد أحسن. وفي الجوهرة قال محمد ثلاث من الدناءة استقراض الخبز والجلوس على باب الحمام والنظر في مرآة الحجام .اهـ. وفي المجتبى باع رغيفاً نقداً برغيفين نسيئة يجوز، ولو كان الرغيفان نقداً والرغيف نسيئة لا يجوز، ولو باع كسيرات الخبز يجوز نقداً ونسيئة كيف كان قوله : (ولا) ربا بين المولى وعبده) لأنه وما في يده ملكه . أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه وأما إذا كان مستغرقاً فيجري الربا بينهما اتفاقاً لعدم الملك عنده للمولى في كسبه كالمكاتب وعندهما لتعلق حق الغير، والتحقيق أنه
His saying: (And olives with oil and sesame with shiraj such that the oil and shiraj are more than what is in the olives and sesame) i.e., the sale is not valid in three cases: first, if it is known that the oil in the olives is more, because of the realized excess of the oil and the pulp; second, if equality is known, because the pulp is without compensation; third, if it is not known whether it is equal, more, or less, then it is not valid according to us (Hanafis) because the suspected excess is like realized out of caution. According to Zufar, it is valid because permissibility is the principle, and invalidity is due to the existence of unrequited excess, so as long as it is not proven, it is not invalid. And the sale is permitted unanimously in the case where it is known that the separate oil is more, so that the excess is by the pulp. And likewise, the sale of walnuts with their oil, milk with its butter, dates with their pits, and everything that has pulp of value, when sold with the pure extract, is not permissible unless the pure extract is more. And if the pulp has no value, like gold dust when sold with gold or silver dust when sold with silver, it is not stipulated that the gold or silver be more than what is in the dust, because the dust has no value, so nothing is placed against it; if it is placed, it is invalid due to riba al-fadl. And in al-Hawi: If one sells wheat with wheat in its ears, it is not permissible; but if one sells a bundle of wheat with wheat by measure or by estimation, it is permissible even if the condition of leaving is not stipulated. End. His saying: (And one borrows bread by weight, not by count) This is according to Abu Yusuf; according to Muhammad, one borrows by both; according to Abu Hanifa, one borrows by neither. And the commentator mentioned that the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf. And in Sharh al-Majma': the fatwa is on the saying of Muhammad. And in Fath al-Qadeer: And I see that the saying of Muhammad is better. And in al-Jawharah, Muhammad said: Three things are of lowliness: borrowing bread, sitting at the door of the bathhouse, and looking into the barber's mirror. End. And in al-Mujtaba: If one sells a loaf for cash in exchange for two loaves on credit, it is permissible; if the two loaves are cash and the one loaf on credit, it is not permissible; and if one sells pieces of bread, it is permissible for cash and on credit however it may be. His saying: (And there is no riba between a master and his slave) because he and what is in his hand are his property. This is absolute, and it is restricted when there is no debt consuming his value and earnings. But if it is consuming, then riba runs between them by agreement, due to the lack of ownership of the master over his earnings, like the mukatab; and according to his two companions, due to the attachment of the right of others. And the verification is...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage addresses several specific issues within the chapter of riba. First, the rule of exchanging olives for oil (or sesame for shiraj/tahini). Since olives contain both oil and pulp (tafl), the exchange is only valid if the separated oil exceeds the oil content of the olives, so that the pulp compensates for the excess. If the oil is less or equal, or if the amounts are unknown, the sale is invalid to avoid riba. Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, differs and allows it based on the principle of permissibility. The same rule applies to walnuts with oil, milk with butter, dates with pits, etc. However, if the pulp has no value (like dust from gold or silver), the condition does not apply. Second, the issue of borrowing bread (qard) can be done by weight or count according to different imams: Abu Yusuf allows by weight only, Muhammad allows both, and Abu Hanifa disallows entirely. The later fatwa varies: al-Hidayah follows Abu Yusuf, but Fath al-Qadeer prefers Muhammad's view. The passage also records a saying of Muhammad that borrowing bread is a lowly act. In the sale of bread, specific rules apply: selling one loaf now for two later is valid (the deferred amount is considered a salam), but the reverse is invalid because the immediate delivery of less and deferral of more creates riba. Selling pieces of bread is always permissible. Third, there is no riba between a master and his slave because the slave's property belongs to the master, so it is like one person dealing with himself. However, if the slave has a debt that consumes his value and earnings, then riba applies because the master's ownership is incomplete. This is the position according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
دين مستغرق (dayn mustaghriq): A debt that consumes the entire value and earnings of the slave.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom, paying in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does riba occur between a master and his slave according to the Hanafi school?
A) Never, because the slave's property is the master's
B) Always, because the slave is a separate person
C) Only when the slave has a debt consuming his value and earnings
D) When the transaction involves food

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of riba applies between master and slave only when the slave's debt outweighs his value and earnings.

Exchanging Olives for Oil: The Hanafi Position and the Zufr Dispute
Commentary explaining the valid and invalid scenarios for bartering oil-rich items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on bartering oil-yielding items and identify the three invalid scenarios and one valid scenario, including the intra-madhhab dispute with Zufr.

Core Idea
When bartering oil-yielding items, the pure extract must exceed the pulp's value to avoid riba; potential excess is treated as actual.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the conditions for bartering items like olives for oil. It distinguishes three scenarios that lead to invalidity due to riba al-fadl and one valid scenario. The commentator also highlights an intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufr, who permits the sale when the quantity is unknown. Understanding this ruling is crucial for ensuring riba-free transactions in commodities with residual value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the core prohibition
Exchanging olives for oil is a barter of the same genus (both involve oil), thus subject to riba al-fadl rules. The act is prohibited if the exchange is unequal or uncertain.
Riba al-fadl applies to spot exchanges of same generic items measured by weight or volume.
2
Distinguish the three invalid scenarios
The sale is invalid in three cases: (1) when the oil in the olives is known to be more than the oil being exchanged, (2) when they are equal, and (3) when the quantity is unknown.
In scenarios 1 and 2, excess or lack of consideration for the pulp causes riba.
3
Understand the role of the pulp (tafl)
The pulp (tafl) is the solid residue after oil extraction. It has value, so its presence must be compensated. The pure oil must be known to be greater than the oil in the olives for the transaction to be valid.
If the pulp had no value (e.g., gold dust), the condition of excess is not required.
4
Note the valid scenario by consensus
The sale is permissible when the separated oil is known to be more than the oil in the olives. The excess is then justifiably assigned to the pulp.
This is the only scenario that avoids riba completely.
5
Examine the dispute with Zufr
Zufr permits the sale when equality/inequality is unknown, citing default permissibility. The Hanafi majority prohibits it as a precaution, treating potential excess as actual riba.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) follows the majority position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والزيتون بالزيت والسمسم بالشيرج حتى يكون الزيت والشيرج أكثر مما في الزيتون والسمسم أي لا يجوز البيع في ثلاث صور الأولى أن يعلم أن الزيت الذي في الزيتون أكثر لتحقق الفضل من الدهن والتفل. الثانية أن يعلم التساوي لخلو التفل عن العوض الثالثة أن لا يعلم أنه مثل أو أكثر أو أقل فلا يصح عندنا لأن الفضل المتوهم كالمتحقق احتياطاً، وعند زفر جاز لأن الجواز هو الأصل والفساد لوجود الفضل الخالي فما لم لا يفسد، ويجوز البيع في صورة بالإجماع أن يعلم أن الزيت المنفصل أكثر ليكون الفضل بالتفل، وكذا بيع الجوز بدهنه واللبن بسمنه والتمر بنواه وكل شيء لتفله قيمة إذا بيع بالخالص منه لا يجوز حتى يكون الخالص أكثر وإن لم يكن لتفله قيمة كتراب الذهب إذا بيع بالذهب أو تراب الفضة إذا بيع بالفضة لا يشترط أن يكون الذهب أو الفضة أكثر مما في التراب لأن التراب لا قيمة له فلا يجعل بإزائه شيء حتى لو جعل فسد لربا الفضل.
His statement: (And olives for oil, and sesame for sesame oil, such that the oil and sesame oil are more than what is in the olives and sesame). That is, the sale is not valid in three scenarios: first, when it is known that the oil in the olives is more, due to the realization of excess from the oil and the pulp; second, when equality is known, because the pulp lacks consideration; third, when it is not known whether it is equal, more, or less — then it is invalid according to us [Hanafis] because the potential excess is treated like actual excess as a precaution. According to Zufr, it is permissible because permissibility is the default, and corruption is due to existing excess without consideration, so as long as [excess] is not certain, it does not corrupt. The sale is permissible in one scenario by consensus: when it is known that the separated oil is more, so that the excess lies in the pulp. Similarly, the sale of walnuts for their oil, milk for its ghee, dates for their pits, and everything whose pulp has value when sold for the pure extract is not permissible unless the pure extract is more. And if its pulp has no value, like gold dust sold for gold, or silver dust sold for silver, it is not required that the gold or silver be more than what is in the dust, because the dust has no value — so nothing is assigned to it, such that if something were assigned, it would be corrupt due to riba al-fadl.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, clarifying the conditions for bartering oil-yielding items like olives for oil. It defines three invalid scenarios and one valid scenario by consensus. The key principle is that the pulp (tafl) has value, so the pure extract must be greater to avoid riba al-fadl. The commentator notes a disagreement with Zufr, who permits the sale when equality is unknown, while the Hanafi school prohibits it as a precaution. The ruling extends to similar items: walnuts for oil, milk for ghee, dates for pits. For items where the pulp has no value (e.g., gold dust), the condition of excess is not required, and assigning any part of the extract to the dust would corrupt the contract.

Key Terms
تفل (tafl): The solid residue or pulp remaining after extracting oil or juice.
ربا الفضل (ribā al-faḍl): Usury of excess in barter transactions of same generic items measured by weight or volume.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sale of olives for oil, which scenario is permissible by consensus?
A) When the oil in the olives is known to be more
B) When the separated oil is known to be more than the oil in the olives
C) When equality is known
D) When the quantity is unknown

Key Takeaway
In bartering oil-yielding items, validity hinges on knowing that the pure extract exceeds the value of the pulp; otherwise, the transaction is invalid due to riba al-fadl.

Selling Wheat in Its Ear vs Cut Wheat
Al-Hawi stating conditions for validity of selling wheat in different forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between selling wheat in its ear (sunbul) and cut wheat (qasil) and state the Hanafi ruling on each.

Core Idea
Wheat in the ear is a different genus; cut wheat is same genus, so riba rules differ.

This passage from Al-Hawi clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi sales law: the status of wheat when it is still in the ear versus when it has been cut. The ruling hinges on whether the wheat is considered the same genus for the purposes of riba. Understanding this distinction prevents unintended riba in agricultural transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Cases
Note the two scenarios: (1) selling wheat in its ear (sunbul) for wheat, and (2) selling cut wheat (qasil) for wheat.
The text uses 'حنطة في سنبلها' and 'قصيل حنطة'.
2
Understand the Genus Concept
In Hanafi fiqh, the presence of straw in the ear is considered a change that makes the item a different genus, thus barter with wheat is not exchange of same genus. Cut wheat, even with some straw, is still considered the same genus as wheat grain.
Straw in the ear is a significant admixture; in qasil, it is incidental.
3
Apply the Riba Rule
For same genus ribawi items, exchange must be equal and immediate. Since wheat in the ear is different genus, the rule does not apply, but the contract becomes invalid because it is not a valid sale of two different genera either? Actually, the impossibility of assessing equality due to straw makes the sale invalid. For qasil, equality can be established by measure or estimation, so it is permissible.
Invalidity is due to gharar (uncertainty) or lack of equivalence.
4
Note the Permissibility Condition
For cut wheat, the sale is permissible by measure (kayl) or by estimation (juzaf), even without stipulating a condition about the straw. The straw does not hinder equivalence.
The text says 'وان لم يشترط الترك' meaning even if no condition is made.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحاوي : وإن باع حنطة بحنطة في سنبلها لم يجز وإن باع قصيل حنطة بحنطة كيلاً وجزافاً جاز وإن لم يشترط الترك اهـ
And in Al-Hawi: If one sells wheat for wheat in its ear, it is not permissible. And if one sells cut wheat (qasil) of wheat for wheat, by measure or by estimation, it is permissible, even if the condition of mixing [with straw] is not stipulated. End.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This is a ruling from Al-Hawi, a Hanafi text. The distinction is between wheat still in the ear (with attached straw) which is considered a different genus due to the mixture, and cut wheat (qasil) which is the harvested grain possibly with some straw but treated as the same genus. The latter is permissible if sold by weight or estimation, without requiring a special condition about the straw.

Key Terms
قصيل (qaṣīl): Cut wheat, i.e., harvested stalks with grain still attached.
سنبل (sunbul): Ear of wheat, the head of the stalk containing grain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Hawi, selling cut wheat (qasil) for wheat is:
A) Invalid unless measured by weight
B) Valid only by estimation
C) Valid whether by measure or estimation
D) Invalid because it is riba

Key Takeaway
Wheat in the ear is a different genus from wheat grain, making barter invalid; cut wheat is the same genus, so barter is permissible with measure or estimation.

Borrowing Bread by Weight vs. Count: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on borrowing bread

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on borrowing bread and evaluate which view is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Bread borrowing sparks a three-way Hanafi dispute on avoiding riba in ribawi goods.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about borrowing bread, a ribawi commodity. The three eponymous imams of the school — Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad — each held a distinct view. The passage also records Muhammad's moral admonition against borrowing bread, which is distinct from the legal ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ribawi status
Recognise that borrowing bread (qard) involves a ribawi commodity (food sold by weight). The dispute centres on how to avoid riba in repayment.
Bread is typically sold by weight due to size variation.
2
Map the three Hanafi positions
List: Abu Hanifa prohibits borrowing bread altogether; Abu Yusuf allows only by weight; Muhammad allows by both weight and count.
Each imam balances the risk of riba against practical need.
3
Trace the underlying reasoning (illa)
The illa is the ambiguity in count: if loaves vary in weight, returning by count may involve unequal value, leading to riba. Weight ensures certainty.
This is a qiyas-based reasoning extending riba rules to borrowing.
4
Examine the fatwa hierarchy
Later authorities differ: some prefer Abu Yusuf (weight only), others Muhammad (both). Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir prefers Muhammad's view.
The mufta bihi is not unanimous; local practice may influence the choice.
5
Separate moral advice from legal ruling
Muhammad's saying that borrowing bread is a sign of lowliness (dana'a) is moral guidance, not a legal prohibition. The legal ruling remains mubah.
Do not conflate akhlaq with fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويستقرض الخبز وزنا لا عدداً) وهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد يستقرض بهما، وعند أبي حنيفة لا يستقرض بهما، وذكر الشارح أن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف. وفي شرح المجمع: الفتوى على قول محمد. وفي فتح القدير : وأنا أرى أن قول محمد أحسن. وفي الجوهرة قال محمد ثلاث من الدناءة استقراض الخبز والجلوس على باب الحمام والنظر في مرآة الحجام .اهـ.
His statement: (And one may borrow bread by weight, not by count). This is according to Abu Yusuf. According to Muhammad, one may borrow by both [weight and count]. According to Abu Hanifa, one may not borrow by either. The commentator mentioned that the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf. In Sharh al-Majma': the fatwa is on Muhammad's saying. In Fath al-Qadir: 'I see that Muhammad's saying is better.' In al-Jawhara: 'Muhammad said: Three things are from lowliness: borrowing bread, sitting at the door of the bathhouse, and looking into the barber's mirror.' End.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage records an intra-Hanafi dispute on borrowing bread. Bread is typically sold by weight due to variations in size, so borrowing by count could lead to riba. Abu Yusuf allows borrowing by weight only; Muhammad allows both; Abu Hanifa disallows both. The commentator notes that fatwa differs: some rely on Abu Yusuf, others on Muhammad. The additional citation from Muhammad himself discourages borrowing bread as a sign of lowliness, but that is moral advice, not a legal ruling.

Key Terms
دناءة (danā'a): Lowliness or meanness; moral disapproval.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only by count
C) By both weight and count
D) It cannot be borrowed at all

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has three positions on borrowing bread; the operative fatwa differs, and Muhammad's moral remark is separate from legal ruling.

Selling One Loaf for Two: Time Consideration in Riba Contracts
Fatawa ruling on credit and spot sales of bread loaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging bread loaves with different quantities and credit terms, and identify when the transaction is valid or invalid due to riba.

Core Idea
In ribawi sales, an increase in quantity is permissible only if paired with a credit term.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from al-Mujtaba regarding the sale of bread loaves. The key principle is that when quantities differ in a ribawi exchange, the transaction must be spot unless the excess is justified as compensation for credit. The ruling distinguishes between whole loaves and broken pieces, illustrating the nuance in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction types
The passage presents two scenarios: (A) one loaf spot for two credit, (B) two loaves spot for one credit. State each clearly.
The third scenario involves broken pieces, which are treated differently.
2
Apply the riba rule
Recall that ribawi items (foods sold by weight/measure) cannot be exchanged in unequal quantities unless spot. Here, bread is ribawi. Determine validity by checking whether the increase is on the credit side or the spot side.
The increase on the credit side is permissible because it compensates for the delay.
3
Analyze the exception for broken pieces
Broken pieces (kusayrat) are considered a different genus from whole loaves. Therefore, the riba restriction does not apply, and any exchange (spot or credit) is valid.
This shows that the Hanafi school looks at the actual commodity category, not just the name.
4
Conclude the operative principle
The principle: in a sale of ribawi items, if quantities differ, the excess must be in exchange for a credit period (or some other compensation) to avoid riba. If the excess is on the spot side, it is riba and invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى باع رغيفاً نقداً برغيفين نسيئة يجوز، ولو كان الرغيفان نقداً والرغيف نسيئة لا يجوز، ولو باع كسيرات الخبز يجوز نقداً ونسيئة كيف كان قوله : (ولا) ربا بين المولى وعبده) لأنه وما في يده ملكه . أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه وأما إذا كان مستغرقاً فيجري الربا بينهما اتفاقاً لعدم الملك عنده للمولى في كسبه كالمكاتب وعندهما لتعلق حق الغير، والتحقيق أنه،
And in al-Mujtaba: he sold one loaf spot for two loaves on credit — it is permissible. Had the two loaves been spot and the one loaf on credit, it would not be permissible. And if he sold broken pieces of bread, it is permissible spot or credit, however it is.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This ruling from al-Mujtaba illustrates the principle that in ribawi transactions, the exchange must be spot when quantities differ. Selling one loaf for two on credit is valid because the increase is in the credit period, not in the item itself. The reverse (one loaf on credit for two spot) is invalid because the delay is on the side of the lesser quantity, which resembles riba. Broken pieces are treated as a different commodity, so no restriction applies.

Key Terms
كسيرات (kusayrāt): Broken pieces of bread; considered a different commodity than whole loaves for riba purposes.
نقداً (naqdan): Spot sale; immediate exchange of goods and payment.
نسيئة (nasī'ah): Credit sale; deferred payment or delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling one loaf spot for two loaves on credit permissible?
A) Because loaves are not ribawi items
B) Because the increase is due to credit, not excess of the same item
C) Because the buyer is paying more
D) Because the seller is generous

Key Takeaway
Selling one loaf spot for two on credit is valid because the extra loaf compensates for the credit; the reverse is invalid (riba). Broken pieces are a different genus, allowing any exchange.

No Riba Between Master and Slave: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Overwhelming Debt
Explaining the absence of riba between master and slave and the intra-Hanafi dispute on overwhelming debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that riba does not apply between master and slave, analyze the intra-madhhab dispute regarding overwhelming debt, and apply the ruling to related cases (ma'dhun, mudabbar, mukatib).

Core Idea
No riba between master and slave because the slave's property is the master's, but overwhelming debt triggers a dispute about creditors' rights.

This lesson explores a unique ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the absence of riba in transactions between a master and his slave. While the general principle is clear—the master owns the slave and all his possessions—a nuance arises when the slave is burdened with debt that exceeds his value and earnings. In such cases, an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) emerges. We will examine the rule, its exceptions, and how later scholars reconciled the positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its rationale
State the general rule: no riba because the master owns the slave and his property.
This is the default position without any debt.
2
Recognize the condition that changes the ruling
Overwhelming debt (mustaghriq) suspends the master's full ownership of earnings, leading to dispute.
The condition is when debt exceeds the slave's value and earnings.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare Abu Hanifa's view (master does not own earnings) with Sahibayn's view (creditors' rights attach).
This is the core ikhtilaf in the passage.
4
Examine the verified position and its practical consequence
According to al-Mi'raj, no riba even with debt, but excess must be returned to creditors.
This reconciles the two views by upholding the general rule while protecting creditors.
5
Apply to related categories: ma'dhun, mudabbar, umm al-walad, mukatib
Determine the ruling for each type of slave based on the principles discussed.
Mudabbar and umm al-walad are like regular slaves; mukatib is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا) ربا بين المولى وعبده) لأنه وما في يده ملكه . أطلقه وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن عليه دين مستغرق لرقبته وكسبه وأما إذا كان مستغرقاً فيجري الربا بينهما اتفاقاً لعدم الملك عنده للمولى في كسبه كالمكاتب وعندهما لتعلق حق الغير، والتحقيق أنه على إطلاقه ولا ربا بينهما وإن كان مديوناً مستغرقاً وإنما يرد الزائد لتعلق حق الغرماء به كما لو أخذ منه شيئاً بغير عقد، كذا في المعراج ولو كان عليه دين غير مستغرق فلا ربا. وفي مأذون المحيط إذا أخذ المولى من كسب المأذون شيئاً ثم لحقه دين سلم للمولى ما أخذ، وإن الأخذ ولو قليلاً لم يسلم، وفائدته لو لحقه آخر رد المولى جميع ما أخذه بخلاف ما إذا أخذ منه ضريبة وليس عليه دين فإنها تسلم له استحساناً، والمدبر وأم الولد كالعبد بخلاف المكاتب.
His statement: (And there is no riba between the master and his slave) because the master owns the slave and all that the slave possesses. This is stated absolutely but is restricted to when the slave has no debt that overwhelms his person (raqabah) and earnings. However, if the debt is overwhelming, riba occurs between them by agreement (ittifaq) because, according to Abu Hanifa, the master does not own the slave's earnings (like a mukatib), and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), due to the attachment of others' rights. The verification is that it is general, and there is no riba between them even if the slave is overwhelmed by debt; the excess is returned only because of the creditors' rights, as if he took something from him without a contract. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And if the slave's debt is not overwhelming, there is no riba. And in Ma'dhun al-Muhit: if the master takes something from the earnings of the ma'dhun (permitted slave), then a debt comes upon the slave, the master retains what he took; but if he takes even a little, it is not retained. The benefit is: if another debt later arises, the master returns all that he took, unlike when he takes a tax (dariba) and there is no debt, for it is retained by istihsan. The mudabbar and umm al-walad are like a slave, unlike the mukatib.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on riba between master and slave. The basic rule is no riba because the slave's property belongs to the master. However, if the slave has an overwhelming debt (debt exceeding his value and earnings), then riba may occur because the master's ownership over the slave's earnings is suspended. There is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the nature of the slave's earnings. The text then quotes al-Mi'raj that even with overwhelming debt, riba does not apply, but the excess must be returned due to creditors' rights. Further details from al-Muhit about the ma'dhun slave and the difference between taking a tax and other takings. Mudabbar and umm al-walad are treated like slaves, but mukatib is different.

Key Terms
مستغرق (mustaghriq): Overwhelming debt that exceeds the slave's value and earnings.
مأذون (ma'dhūn): A slave permitted to trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the main ruling, is there riba between a master and his slave if the slave has a debt that does not overwhelm his value?
A) Yes, riba always occurs
B) No, no riba
C) Only if the debt is overwhelming
D) Only if the sale is on credit

Key Takeaway
The general rule of no riba between master and slave holds unless the slave's debt overwhelms his value, in which case the excess must be returned to creditors, reflecting an intra-Hanafi dispute about the nature of ownership.
