Riba with Harbi in Dar al-Harb: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on riba with Harbi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on riba with a Harbi in Dar al-Harb, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving musta'min and those who become Muslim in Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
In Dar al-Harb, Harbi's property is mubah, so riba is permissible per Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Riba is a major prohibition in Islamic law, but the Hanafi school carves out a significant exception in the context of war. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a Muslim may engage in riba transactions with a Harbi (non-Muslim enemy) in Dar al-Harb. The majority view, held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, permits it based on a specific hadith and the principle that Harbi wealth is unprotected. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing the general prohibition stands. We will walk through the evidence and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is riba between a Muslim and a Harbi in Dar al-Harb. The default ruling in the Hanafi school is that riba is haram, but this passage addresses an exception.
Riba includes any unequal exchange of ribawi items.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that such riba is permissible. Abu Yusuf prohibits it. Identify their respective positions.
The dispute is over the applicability of the general riba prohibition.
3
Examine the dalil for the majority
The hadith 'لا ربا بين المسلم والحربي في دار الحرب' is cited. Also, the rationale (ta'lil) that Harbi wealth is mubah (permissible to take) because they are not under Islamic protection.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut; the reasoning uses istihsan.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf relies on the general Qur'anic and hadith evidence prohibiting riba universally. The Hanafi response is that the specific hadith limits the general texts to Dar al-Harb.
The methodology involves specifying (takhsis) a general prohibition with a specific hadith.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a Muslim takes riba from a Harbi in Dar al-Harb, the transaction is valid and permissible according to the majority; Abu Yusuf would rule it invalid and prohibited.
The ruling also applies to similar contracts like selling wine, pork, or gambling with Harbis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ربا بين المولى وعبده ولا بين المسلم والحربي ثمة على إطلاقه ولا ربا بينهما وإن كان مديوناً مستغرقاً وإنما يرد الزائد لتعلق حق الغرماء به كما لو أخذ منه شيئاً بغير عقد، كذا في المعراج ولو كان عليه دين غير مستغرق فلا ربا. وفي مأذون المحيط إذا أخذ المولى من كسب المأذون شيئاً ثم لحقه دين سلم للمولى ما أخذ، وإن الأخذ ولو قليلاً لم يسلم، وفائدته لو لحقه آخر رد المولى جميع ما أخذه بخلاف ما إذا أخذ منه ضريبة وليس عليه دين فإنها تسلم له استحساناً، والمدبر وأم الولد كالعبد بخلاف المكاتب. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لا ربا بين المتفاوضين وشريكي العنان إذا تبايعا من مال الشركة وإن كان من غيره جرى بينهما .

قوله: (ولا) بين الحربي والمسلم ثمة أي لا ربا بينهما في دار الحرب عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف. وفي البناية وكذا إذا باع خمراً أو خنزيراً أو ميتة أو قامرهم وأخذ المال كل ذلك يحل له. ولهما الحديث لا ربا بين المسلم والحربي في دار الحرب ولأن مالهم مباح وبعقد الأمان منهم لم يصر معصوماً إلا أنه التزم أن لا يتعرض لهم بغدر ولا لما في أيديهم بدون رضاهم فإذا أخذ برضاهم أخذ مالاً مباحاً بلا غدر فيملكه بحكم الإباحة السابقة إلا أنه لا يخفى أنه إنما اقتضى حل مباشرة العقد إذا كان الزيادة ينالها المسلم والربا أعم من يشمل ما إذا كان الدرهمان من جهة المسلم أو من جهة الكافر وجواب المسألة بالحل عام في الوجهين؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وحكم من أسلم في دار الحرب ولم يهاجر كالحربي عند أبي حنيفة لأن ماله غير معصوم عنده فيجوز للمسلم الربا معه وأما إذا هاجر إلينا ثم عاد إليهم لم يجز الربا معه لكونه أحرز ماله بدارنا فكان من أهل دار الإسلام كذا في الجوهرة. وفي المجتبى معزياً إلى الكفاية مستأمن منا باشر مع رجل مسلماً كان أو ذمياً في دارهم أو من أسلم هناك شيئاً من العقود التي لا تجوز فيما بيننا كالربويات وبيع الميتة جاز عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف اهـ . والله تعالى أعلم .
And there is no riba between the master and his slave, nor between the Muslim and the Harbi there in absolute terms, and there is no riba between them even if he is a debtor whose debts consume his wealth, but he returns the excess because the right of creditors is attached to it, just as if he took something from him without a contract. Likewise in al-Mi‘raj: and if he had a debt that does not consume his wealth, then there is no riba. In Ma’dhan al-Muhit: if the master takes something from the earnings of the ma’dhan, then a debt befalls him, what the master took is valid for him, and if the taking was even small, it is not valid. Its benefit: if another debt befalls him, the master returns all that he took, contrary to if he took a tax from him and he had no debt, then it is valid for him by istihsan. And the mudabbar and umm al-walad are like the slave, contrary to the mukatab. And the author indicated that there is no riba between the mutafawidan and the partners of ‘inan if they trade from the partnership capital, but if from other than it, riba occurs between them.

His statement: (And not) between the Harbi and the Muslim there, meaning there is no riba between them in Dar al-Harb according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. In al-Binaya: and likewise if he sold wine, or a pig, or carrion, or gambled with them and took the money, all of that is permissible for him. The proof for the two is the hadith: 'There is no riba between the Muslim and the Harbi in Dar al-Harb,' and because their wealth is mubah, and by a contract of aman from them it does not become protected, except that he committed not to betray them nor to what is in their hands without their consent. So if he took with their consent, he took mubah wealth without treachery, so he owns it by virtue of the previous ibaha, except that it is not hidden that it only necessitated the permissibility of directly conducting the contract when the increase is obtained by the Muslim, and riba is more general, including when the two dirhams are from the Muslim or from the disbeliever. The answer to the issue is permissibility generally in both aspects; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And the ruling of one who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb and does not emigrate is like the Harbi according to Abu Hanifa, because his wealth is not protected in his view, so it is permissible for the Muslim to engage in riba with him. But if he emigrated to us then returned to them, riba with him is not permissible because he secured his wealth in our Dar, making him among the people of Dar al-Islam; thus in al-Jawhara. And in al-Mujtaba, citing al-Kifaya: a musta’min from us who conducts with a man, whether Muslim or dhimmi, in their Dar, or one who became Muslim there, some contracts that are not valid among us, like ribawi transactions and sale of carrion, is permissible according to the two, contrary to Abu Yusuf. End. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on riba between a Muslim and a Harbi in Dar al-Harb. The text first clarifies that there is no riba between a master and his slave, and between a Muslim and a Harbi in Dar al-Harb. It then discusses the case where the Harbi is indebted: if his debts consume his wealth, the Muslim must return any excess taken; if not, no riba applies. The commentary cites al-Muhit on the ma’dhan: what the master takes from the slave’s earnings is valid if no debt follows, but if a debt arises later, the master returns everything. The passage then addresses the dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that riba is not prohibited between a Muslim and a Harbi in Dar al-Harb, citing the hadith and the principle that Harbi wealth is mubah. Abu Yusuf disagrees. The text elaborates on the ruling's scope: it applies only when the Muslim gets the increase, but the general permissibility covers both directions. It also covers the cases of a Muslim who becomes Muslim in Dar al-Harb without hijrah (treated as Harbi) and one who emigrates then returns (treated as resident). Finally, it notes that a musta’min from us can conduct invalid contracts like riba with Harbis according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): Increase in a loan or sale of a ribawi item without equivalent compensation; prohibited in Islam but excepted here for Harbi.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Muslim protection and is in a state of war with Muslims; his property is considered mubah (permissible to take) in Dar al-Harb.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Muslim rule where hostilities are possible; special rulings apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for the permissibility of riba with a Harbi according to the majority Hanafi view?
A) The transaction must take place in Dar al-Harb.
B) The other party must be a Harbi (non-Muslim enemy).
C) The Harbi must not be indebted.
D) The Muslim must obtain the excess with the Harbi's consent.

Key Takeaway
In Dar al-Harb, riba with a Harbi is permissible according to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), based on a specific hadith and the principle that Harbi wealth is mubah.

The Concept of Haqq and Classification of Rights
Definition of 'haqq' and its categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'haqq' in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between the four categories of rights (Huquq Allah, Huquq al-'Ibad, and mixed).

Core Idea
Haqq is a right; rights are of four types: Allah's, servants', and two mixed categories where one predominates.

The term 'haqq' appears frequently in fiqh, from prayer obligations to property rights. Before diving into rulings, we must understand what 'haqq' means and how Hanafi jurists categorize rights. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and technical definitions from the passage, then introduces the fourfold classification that determines whether a right can be forgiven, dropped, or enforced by the state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of 'haqq'
Read the definitions from al-Misbah, al-Binaya, and Sharh al-Manar. Note that 'haqq' is the opposite of falsehood and that truth is also called haqq.
The word has multiple nuances; our focus is the fiqhi sense: a right or entitlement.
2
Understand the technical fiqhi meaning
The passage quotes al-Kirmani stating that Allah is al-Haqq in reality; applying haqq to others is metaphorical. In fiqh, haqq is what a person deserves legally.
3
List the four categories of rights
The usuliyyun divided rights into: (1) pure rights of Allah, (2) pure rights of servants, (3) mixed with Allah's right predominant, (4) mixed with servant's right predominant.
Each category has different rules regarding forgiveness, dropping, and enforcement.
4
Apply the categories to examples
Give an example for each: (1) prayer (haqq Allah), (2) debts (haqq al-'abd), (3) hadd of qadhf (mixed with Allah's right dominant), (4) qisas (mixed with servant's right dominant).
These examples are from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحقوق
الحقوق جمع حق. وفي المصباح الحق خلاف الباطل وهو مصدر حق الشيء من بابي ضرب وقتل إذا وجب وثبت ولهذا يقال لمرافق الدار حقوقها .اهـ. وفي البناية : الحق ما يستحقه الرجل وله معان أخر منها الحق ضد الباطل .اهـ. وفي شرح المنار للسيد نكركار : الحق هو الشيء الموجود من كل وجه ولا ريب في وجوده ومنه قوله عليه السلام «السحر حق والعين حق اهـ. وفي شرح البخاري للكرماني: الحق حقيقة هو لأنه الموجود حقيقة بمعنى لم يسبق بعدم ولم يلحقه عدم وإطلاق الحق على غيره مجاز ولذا ورد في الحديث اللهم أنت الحق ووعدك الحق وقولك الحق بالتعريف في الثلاثة ولقاؤك حق والجنة حق والنار حق والساعة حق بالتنكير اهـ وذكر الأصوليون أن الأحكام أربعة حقوق الله تعالى خالصة وحقوق العباد خالصة وما اجتمعا فيه وحق الله تعالى غالب كحد القذف، وما اجتمعا فيه وحق العباد غالب كالقصاص. قالوا: والمراد من حق الله تعالى ما تعلق نفعه بالعموم وإنما نسب إلى الله تعظيماً لأنه متعال عن أن ينتفع بشيء ولا يجوز أن يكون حقاً له تعالى بجهة التخليق لأن الكل سواء في ذلك قوله : (العلو لا يدخل بشراء بيت بكل حق يعني إذا اشترى بيتاً فوقه بيت لا يدخل فيه العلو ولو قال بكل حق هو له ما لم ينص عليه لأن البيت اسم لمسقف واحد يصلح للبيتوتة والعلو مثله والشيء لا يكون تبعاً لمثله. وفي المصباح : علو الدار وغيرها خلاف السفل لبضم العين وكسرها اهـ. وأورد المستعير له أن ما لا يختلف والمكاتب له أن يكاتب عبده فأجيب بأن ذلك ليس بطريق يعير الاستتباع بل لما ملك المستعير المنفعة بغير بدل كان له أن يملك ما ملك كذلك، والمكاتب بعقد الكتابة لما صار أحق بمكاسبه كان له ذلك لأن كتابة عبده من أكسابه .
Chapter of Rights. Rights (huquq) is the plural of haqq. In al-Misbah: al-haqq is the opposite of falsehood (al-batil), and it is a masdar from the verbs haqqa (to be due) and haqqa (to establish), thus one says 'the amenities of the house are its rights.' End. In al-Binaya: al-haqq is what a man deserves, and it has other meanings, including al-haqq as opposed to al-batil. End. In Sharh al-Manar by Sayyid Nukarkar: al-haqq is the thing that exists from every aspect, and there is no doubt in its existence, including his statement (peace be upon him): 'Sorcery is real (haqq), and the evil eye is real (haqq).' End. In Sharh al-Bukhari by al-Kirmani: al-haqq in reality is He (Allah) because He exists in reality, i.e., not preceded by non-existence nor followed by non-existence, and applying al-haqq to others is metaphorical. Thus in the hadith: 'O Allah, You are al-Haqq, and Your promise is al-haqq, and Your statement is al-haqq' with definite article in the three, and 'Your meeting is haqq, and Paradise is haqq, and Hell is haqq, and the Hour is haqq' with indefinite article. End. And the usuliyyun mentioned that rulings are four: pure rights of Allah, pure rights of servants, what combines both where Allah's right is predominant like the hadd of qadhf, and what combines both where the servant's right is predominant like qisas. They said: what is meant by Allah's right is that its benefit pertains to the public, and it is attributed to Allah as an exaltation because He is exalted above benefiting from anything, and it is not permissible for it to be a right for Him in the sense of creation because all are equal in that. His statement: (The upper floor does not enter with the purchase of a house with every right) means that if one buys a house above which there is a house, the upper floor is not included, even if he said 'with every right for him' unless he explicitly stipulates it, because 'house' is a name for a single roofed space suitable for dwelling, and the upper floor is like it, and a thing cannot be a subordinate to its like. In al-Misbah: 'ulu of the house and others is the opposite of sufl (lower part) with the 'ayn vowel being u or i. End. And the objector raised that the musta'ir (borrower) can lend what does not differ, and the mukatib can write his slave; he was answered that this is not by way of requiring subordination, rather because when the musta'ir owned the usufruct without compensation, he could own what he owned similarly, and the mukatib by the contract of kitaba, since he became more entitled to his earnings, he could do that because writing his slave is from his earnings.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Rights (Huquq). It first defines the term 'haqq' linguistically and technically, citing multiple sources. It distinguishes between rights of Allah and rights of servants, and their combinations. The main ruling: when one buys a house, the upper floor ('ulu) is not automatically included in the sale, even if the buyer says 'with every right', unless explicitly stipulated. The reason is that 'house' refers to a single structure; the upper floor is a separate entity, and a thing cannot be subordinate to its like. The commentary addresses an objection: why can a borrower lend the same item, and a mukatab write his slave? The response is that those are different cases, not based on subordination but on ownership of the usufruct or earnings.

Key Terms
حق (haqq): A right or entitlement; also the truth; in fiqh, it is what a person deserves legally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a mixed right where Allah's right is predominant according to the passage?
A) Repayment of a loan
B) Hadd of qadhf (false accusation)
C) Qisas (retaliation)
D) Zakat

Key Takeaway
The term 'haqq' in fiqh refers to a legally recognized claim, and the Hanafi school divides rights into four categories based on whose interest predominates—a classification that determines enforceability and dispensability.

Ruling on Including the Upper Floor ('Ulu) in a House Sale
Ruling on inclusion of upper floor in house sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the upper floor ('ulu) is not automatically included in a house sale and explain the reasoning using the principle that a thing cannot be subordinate to its like.

Core Idea
The upper floor is not included in a house sale because each floor is a separate entity; a thing cannot be subordinate to its like.

When you buy a house, does that include the floor above? This is a practical question with a clear answer in Hanafi fiqh. The passage provides the ruling and its logical basis, grounded in the definition of 'house' and the principle of subordination. It also addresses an objection to defend the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the exact wording: 'The upper floor does not enter with the purchase of a house with every right.' This means even if the buyer says 'with every right', the upper floor is excluded unless explicitly mentioned.
The phrase 'bikull haqq' does not automatically include the upper floor.
2
Define key terms: 'bayt' and 'ulu'
From al-Misbah: 'bayt' is a single roofed space suitable for dwelling; 'ulu' is the opposite of sufl (lower part), meaning the upper floor.
Each is a separate entity.
3
Explain the qiyas reasoning
The author argues: the house (lower floor) is a roofed space; the upper floor is like it. A thing cannot be a subordinate (tabi') to its like (mithl). Therefore, the upper floor is not automatically included.
The principle is that an item is not subordinate to an equal item; subordination requires that one is part of or accessory to the other.
4
Address the objection
The objector raises two cases: (1) a borrower can lend the borrowed item (musta'ir), and (2) a mukatib can write his slave. If these are allowed, why can't the buyer claim the upper floor? The answer: these cases are based on ownership of usufruct or earnings, not on subordination.
The objection is refuted by distinguishing the legal basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحقوق
الحقوق جمع حق. وفي المصباح الحق خلاف الباطل وهو مصدر حق الشيء من بابي ضرب وقتل إذا وجب وثبت ولهذا يقال لمرافق الدار حقوقها .اهـ. وفي البناية : الحق ما يستحقه الرجل وله معان أخر منها الحق ضد الباطل .اهـ. وفي شرح المنار للسيد نكركار : الحق هو الشيء الموجود من كل وجه ولا ريب في وجوده ومنه قوله عليه السلام «السحر حق والعين حق اهـ. وفي شرح البخاري للكرماني: الحق حقيقة هو لأنه الموجود حقيقة بمعنى لم يسبق بعدم ولم يلحقه عدم وإطلاق الحق على غيره مجاز ولذا ورد في الحديث اللهم أنت الحق ووعدك الحق وقولك الحق بالتعريف في الثلاثة ولقاؤك حق والجنة حق والنار حق والساعة حق بالتنكير اهـ وذكر الأصوليون أن الأحكام أربعة حقوق الله تعالى خالصة وحقوق العباد خالصة وما اجتمعا فيه وحق الله تعالى غالب كحد القذف، وما اجتمعا فيه وحق العباد غالب كالقصاص. قالوا: والمراد من حق الله تعالى ما تعلق نفعه بالعموم وإنما نسب إلى الله تعظيماً لأنه متعال عن أن ينتفع بشيء ولا يجوز أن يكون حقاً له تعالى بجهة التخليق لأن الكل سواء في ذلك قوله : (العلو لا يدخل بشراء بيت بكل حق يعني إذا اشترى بيتاً فوقه بيت لا يدخل فيه العلو ولو قال بكل حق هو له ما لم ينص عليه لأن البيت اسم لمسقف واحد يصلح للبيتوتة والعلو مثله والشيء لا يكون تبعاً لمثله. وفي المصباح : علو الدار وغيرها خلاف السفل لبضم العين وكسرها اهـ. وأورد المستعير له أن ما لا يختلف والمكاتب له أن يكاتب عبده فأجيب بأن ذلك ليس بطريق يعير الاستتباع بل لما ملك المستعير المنفعة بغير بدل كان له أن يملك ما ملك كذلك، والمكاتب بعقد الكتابة لما صار أحق بمكاسبه كان له ذلك لأن كتابة عبده من أكسابه .
Chapter of Rights. Rights (huquq) is the plural of haqq. In al-Misbah: al-haqq is the opposite of falsehood (al-batil), and it is a masdar from the verbs haqqa (to be due) and haqqa (to establish), thus one says 'the amenities of the house are its rights.' End. In al-Binaya: al-haqq is what a man deserves, and it has other meanings, including al-haqq as opposed to al-batil. End. In Sharh al-Manar by Sayyid Nukarkar: al-haqq is the thing that exists from every aspect, and there is no doubt in its existence, including his statement (peace be upon him): 'Sorcery is real (haqq), and the evil eye is real (haqq).' End. In Sharh al-Bukhari by al-Kirmani: al-haqq in reality is He (Allah) because He exists in reality, i.e., not preceded by non-existence nor followed by non-existence, and applying al-haqq to others is metaphorical. Thus in the hadith: 'O Allah, You are al-Haqq, and Your promise is al-haqq, and Your statement is al-haqq' with definite article in the three, and 'Your meeting is haqq, and Paradise is haqq, and Hell is haqq, and the Hour is haqq' with indefinite article. End. And the usuliyyun mentioned that rulings are four: pure rights of Allah, pure rights of servants, what combines both where Allah's right is predominant like the hadd of qadhf, and what combines both where the servant's right is predominant like qisas. They said: what is meant by Allah's right is that its benefit pertains to the public, and it is attributed to Allah as an exaltation because He is exalted above benefiting from anything, and it is not permissible for it to be a right for Him in the sense of creation because all are equal in that. His statement: (The upper floor does not enter with the purchase of a house with every right) means that if one buys a house above which there is a house, the upper floor is not included, even if he said 'with every right for him' unless he explicitly stipulates it, because 'house' is a name for a single roofed space suitable for dwelling, and the upper floor is like it, and a thing cannot be a subordinate to its like. In al-Misbah: 'ulu of the house and others is the opposite of sufl (lower part) with the 'ayn vowel being u or i. End. And the objector raised that the musta'ir (borrower) can lend what does not differ, and the mukatib can write his slave; he was answered that this is not by way of requiring subordination, rather because when the musta'ir owned the usufruct without compensation, he could own what he owned similarly, and the mukatib by the contract of kitaba, since he became more entitled to his earnings, he could do that because writing his slave is from his earnings.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Rights (Huquq). It first defines the term 'haqq' linguistically and technically, citing multiple sources. It distinguishes between rights of Allah and rights of servants, and their combinations. The main ruling: when one buys a house, the upper floor ('ulu) is not automatically included in the sale, even if the buyer says 'with every right', unless explicitly stipulated. The reason is that 'house' refers to a single structure; the upper floor is a separate entity, and a thing cannot be subordinate to its like. The commentary addresses an objection: why can a borrower lend the same item, and a mukatab write his slave? The response is that those are different cases, not based on subordination but on ownership of the usufruct or earnings.

Key Terms
علو ('ulu): The upper floor or upper part of a house; opposite of sufl (lower part).
بيع (bay'): Sale; contract transferring ownership of property for consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the upper floor not included in the sale of a house even with the phrase 'with every right'?
A) Because the upper floor is more valuable than the lower
B) Because 'house' refers to a single roofed space, and the upper floor is a separate entity
C) Because the seller did not intend to sell it
D) Because local custom excludes upper floors

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule is clear: buying a house does not include the upper floor; each floor is a separate property unless the contract explicitly states otherwise.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Inclusion of Upper Floors and Appurtenances in Sale of Houses
Explains which appurtenances, especially the upper floor, are included when selling different types of residences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for automatic inclusion of the upper floor when buying a bayt, manzil, or dar, and apply local custom to determine inclusion of other appurtenances.

Core Idea
The upper floor is a separate entity in a bayt, conditional in a manzil, and automatic in a dar—custom governs.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a critical nuance in property sales: whether the upper floor (uluww) and other appurtenances are included in the sale depends on the specific term used for the property (bayt, manzil, dar) and local custom. Understanding these defaults prevents disputes and ensures that contracts reflect the true intention of the parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property type
Determine whether the contract uses bayt, manzil, or dar. Each triggers a different default for inclusion of the upper floor.
2
Apply the default rule for the upper floor
For bayt: not included unless explicit. For manzil: included only with specific phrases (e.g., 'bi-kulli haqq'). For dar: included automatically even without mention.
The text specifies: 'العلو لا يدخل بشراء بيت... ودخل بشراء دار'
3
Check for explicit phrases
If the buyer uses 'bi-kulli haqq' or equivalent, it may override the default. For dar, this is redundant; for bayt, it becomes necessary.
4
Consider other appurtenances
Lavatory, well, trees: those connected to the building are included in dar; external items depend on size and custom.
The key enters by istihsan, not qiyas.
5
Apply local custom ('urf)
Custom can change the default rulings. The author notes that in his time and place, the upper floor entered even without explicit mention in dar, due to custom.
Al-Kafi and al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya support reliance on custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحقوق
العلو لا يدخل بشراء بيت بكل حق وبشراء منزل إلا بكل حق هو له أو بمرافقه أو بكل قليل وكثير هو فيه أو منه ودخل بشراء دار كالكنيف لا الظلة إلا بكل حق ولا يدخل الطريق والمسيل والشرب إلا بنحو كل حق. قال: ويمكن تصحيح عبارة المؤلف بأن يجعل قوله مسلماً كان أو ذمياً عائداً إلى قوله مستأمن لا إلى رجل. قوله: (ودخل بشراء دار) أي العلو بشراء دار وإن لم يذكر شيئاً من ذلك لأن الدار اسم لما أدير عليه الحدود من الحائط، ويشتمل على بيوت ومنازل وصحن غير مسقف والعلو من أجزائه فيدخل فيه من غير ذكر. وفي البناية: الدار لغة اسم لقطعة أرض ضربت لها الحدود وميزت عما يجاورها بإدارة خط عليها فبني في بعضها دون البعض ليجمع فيها مرافق الصحراء للاسترواح ومنافع الأبنية للإسكان وغير ذلك، ولا فرق بين ما إذا كانت الأبنية بالماء والتراب أو بالخيام والقباب اهـ قوله: (كالكنيف) أي كما يدخل بشراء الدار وإن لم يصرح به لأن الكنيف منها، وكذا يدخل بئر الماء والأشجار التي صحنها والبستان الداخل فأما الخارج فإن كان أكبر منها أو مثلها لا يدخل إلا بشرط، وإن كان أصغر منها يدخل لأنه زماننا دخولها بمجرد العقد بدون قوله كل حق ولا يفهم العاقدان سوى ذلك، فمقتضى ما مر في مسألة للعلو عن الكافي دخول هذه المذكورات وإن لم يقل بكل حق لأن عرف زماننا دخول ذلك لا سيما الشرب. ثم رأيت في الذخيرة البرهانية قال: فالأصل أن ما كان في الدار من البناء أو كان متصلاً بالبناء يدخل في بيع الدار من غير ذكر بطريق التبعية، وما لا يكون متصلاً بالبناء لا يدخل الدار من غير ذكر إلا إذا كان شيئاً جرى العرف فيه فيما بين الناس أن البائع لا يمنعه عن المشتري، فحينئذ يدخل وإن لم يذكره في البيع والمفتاح يدخل استحساناً ولا يدخل قياساً لأنه غير
Chapter of Rights. The upper floor does not enter by buying a house (bayt) with every right, and by buying a dwelling (manzil) except with every right that belongs to him or with its appurtenances or with every small and great that is in it or from it. And it enters by buying a courtyard house (dar) like the lavatory, not the canopy except with every right. And the road, watercourse, and right to drink do not enter except with something like every right. He said: And his statement (and enters by buying a dar) i.e., the upper floor enters by buying a dar even if he does not mention anything of that, because dar is a name for what boundaries are drawn around it from the wall, and it includes houses and dwellings and an uncovered courtyard, and the upper floor is part of it, so it enters without mention. In al-Binaya: dar linguistically is a name for a piece of land whose boundaries are drawn and distinguished from what adjoins it by drawing a line around it, so some of it is built and some not, to gather the facilities of the open space for leisure and the benefits of buildings for dwelling and other things, and there is no difference between whether the buildings are made of water and clay or of tents and domes. He said: (like the lavatory) i.e., just as it enters by buying a dar even if not explicitly stated because the lavatory is part of it, and similarly enter the well of water and the trees that are in its courtyard and the inner garden. As for the outside, if it is larger or equal to it, it does not enter except by condition, and if it is smaller than it, it enters because in our time it enters by the mere contract without saying 'every right' and the two contracting parties understand only that. So according to what passed in the issue of the upper floor from al-Kafi, these mentioned things enter even if he does not say 'with every right' because the custom of our time is their entry, especially the right to drink. Then I saw in al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya: the principle is that what is in the dar of building or is connected to the building enters in the sale of the dar without mention by way of appurtenance, and what is not connected to the building does not enter the dar without mention unless it is something where custom among people is that the seller does not prevent it from the buyer, then it enters even if he does not mention it in the sale. And the key enters by istihsan and does not enter by qiyas because it is not...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the chapter on rights (huquq) in sales. It clarifies which appurtenances are automatically included when selling different types of residential properties, focusing on the upper floor (al-uluww). The ruling is that when buying a bayt (single room or house), the upper floor is not included unless explicitly stated with a phrase like 'with every right.' When buying a manzil (dwelling, which resembles both a bayt and a dar), the upper floor is included only if the buyer uses one of three phrases: 'with every right that belongs to him,' 'with its appurtenances,' or 'with every small and great that is in it or from it.' When buying a dar (courtyard house), the upper floor is automatically included even without mention because the dar encompasses all its parts. The text also distinguishes between appurtenances that are part of the structure (lavatory, well) and those that are not (canopy, road, watercourse). The commentary relies heavily on custom ('urf), noting that in the author's locality (Kufa), these rulings applied, but in other regions and times, the custom may differ, so the rulings should be adapted accordingly. The author cites al-Kafi and al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya to support the principle that whatever is customary to include should be included, even if not mentioned, based on istihsan.

Key Terms
علو (uluww): The upper floor or upper apartment of a building.
بيت (bayt): A single room or house with a roof suitable for dwelling.
منزل (manzil): A dwelling that includes multiple rooms, a covered courtyard, and kitchen; similar to both bayt and dar.
دار (dar): A courtyard house bounded by walls, including an uncovered courtyard and buildings.
حق (haqq): A right (like right of way, watercourse) that is appurtenant to the property.
مرافق (marafiq): Appurtenances or facilities of the house, such as kitchen, lavatory, and watercourses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When buying a 'dar' (courtyard house), is the upper floor (uluww) automatically included in the sale even if not mentioned?
A) Yes, because the dar includes all its parts by default.
B) No, it must be explicitly mentioned with 'bi-kulli haqq'.
C) Only if the buyer is a Muslim.
D) It depends on whether the seller is a Muslim or dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, the term 'dar' automatically includes the upper floor and connected appurtenances, unlike 'bayt' and 'manzil', but local custom may override these defaults.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Inclusion of Upper Floor, Privy, and Appurtenances in Sale of a House
Commentary on matn explaining inclusion of upper floor and privy in sale of house with custom considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which appurtenances are automatically included in the Hanafi sale of a house (dar) and explain how custom (urf) can override the default ruling.

Core Idea
Upper floor and privy enter the sale of a house automatically; the key enters by istihsan, not qiyas.

In Hanafi sales law, what exactly transfers when you buy a house? The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarify that certain appurtenances are included by default based on the term used (dar vs. manzil) and the prevailing custom. This lesson unpacks those rulings and the important role of custom in shaping the sale contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms used in the sale
Recognize that the Arabic terms dar (house), manzil (residence), and bayt (dwelling) have different implications for what is included by default.
The commentary explains that dar linguistically includes the upper floor, while manzil does not unless one of three special phrases is used.
2
Note the default inclusions for dar
For a sale using the term dar, the upper floor (uluww) and privy (kanif) are included without explicit mention. Also, wells, trees in the courtyard, and inner gardens are included as appurtenances.
This is based on the linguistic definition of dar as an enclosed area with buildings.
3
Understand the role of custom (urf)
The commentary quotes al-Kafi that the original ruling was based on Kufan custom; in later eras, the upper floor enters in all types of residential sales by custom. The principle 'ahkam are based on custom' is established.
Custom can override the default linguistic inclusion, so local practice must be checked.
4
Examine the key (miftah) ruling via istihsan
By qiyas, the key should not be included because it is not attached to the building. However, by istihsan, because people customarily include the key, it is included.
This shows how custom can produce a ruling contrary to strict analogy.
5
Recognize the definitional nuance between huquq and marafiq
Rights (huquq) are appurtenances like watercourses and roads. Amenities (marafiq) are defined differently: Abu Yusuf says usufructs, while Zahir al-Riwayah equates marafiq with rights.
This intra-Hanafi difference does not affect the core inclusion ruling but shows legal interpretation diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب الحقوق
قليل وكثير هو فيه أو منه ودخل بشراء دار كالكنيف لا الظلة إلا بكل حق ولا يدخل الطريق
أي لا يدخل العلو بشراء منزل إلا أن يقول المشتري لفظاً من الثلاثة لأن المنزل له شبه بالدار وبالبيت لأنه اسم لما يشتمل على بيوت وصحن مسقف ومطبخ يسكن فيه الرجل بأهله ضرب قصور فيه فإنه ليس فيه اصطبل فلشبه الدار يدخل بذكر التوابع، ولشبه البيت لا يدخل من غير ذكر توفيراً عليهما حظهما . وفي الكافي : إن هذا التفصيل مبني على عرف الكوفة وفي عرفنا يدخل العلو في الكل، سواء باع باسم البيت أو المنزل أو الدار، والأحكام تبتني على العرف فيعتبر في كل إقليم وفي كل عصر عرف أهله. وفي الذخيرة: أعلم أن الحق في العادة يذكر فيما هو تبع للمبيع ولا بد للمبيع منه ولا يقصد إلا لأجل المبيع كالطريق والشرب للأرض والمرافق عبارة عما يرتفق به ويختص بما هو من التوابع كالشرب ومسيل الماء. وقوله كل قليل وكثير يذكر على وجه المبالغة في إسقاط حق البائع عن المبيع مما يتصل بالمبيع اهـ. وفي المصباح المرافق جميع مرفق بكسر الميم وفتح الفاء لا غير كالمطبخ والكنيف ونحوه على التشبيه باسم الآلة بخلاف المرفق في الوضوء فإن فيه لغتين فتح الميم وكسر الفاء كمسجد وبالعكس، وكذا المرفق بمعنى ما ارتفقت به .اهـ. فالحاصل أن المرفق مطلقاً فيه لغتان إلا مرفق الدار. وفي جامع الفصولين من الفصل السابع: وما يذكر في دعوى العقار من قوله بحقوقه ومرافقة فحقوقه عبارة عن مسيل الماء وطريق وغيره وفاقاً ومرافقة عند أبي يوسف عبارة عن منافع الدار ، وفي ظاهر الرواية المرافق هي الحقوق اهـ قوله : ( ودخل بشراء (دار) أي العلو بشراء دار وإن لم يذكر شيئاً . من ذلك لأن الدار اسم لما أدير عليه الحدود من الحائط، ويشتمل على بيوت ومنازل وصحن غير مسقف والعلو من أجزائه فيدخل فيه من غير ذكر. وفي البناية : الدار لغة اسم لقطعة أرض ضربت لها الحدود وميزت عما يجاورها بإدارة خط عليها فبني في بعضها دون البعض ليجمع فيها مرافق الصحراء للاسترواح ومنافع الأبنية للإسكان وغير ذلك، ولا فرق بين ما إذا كانت الأبنية بالماء والتراب أو بالخيام والقباب اهـ قوله : ( كالكنيف) أي كما يدخل بشراء الدار وإن لم يصرح به لأن الكنيف منها، وكذا يدخل بئر الماء والأشجار التي صحنها والبستان الداخل فأما الخارج فإن كان أكبر منها أو مثلها لا يدخل إلا بشرط، وإن كان أصغر منها يدخل لأنه
زماننا دخولها بمجرد العقد بدون قوله كل حق ولا يفهم العاقدان سوى ذلك، فمقتضى ما مر في مسألة للعلو عن الكافي دخول هذه المذكورات وإن لم يقل بكل حق لأن عرف زماننا دخول ذلك لا سيما الشرب. ثم رأيت في الذخيرة البرهانية قال: فالأصل أن ما كان في الدار من البناء أو كان متصلاً بالبناء يدخل في بيع الدار من غير ذكر بطريق التبعية، وما لا يكون متصلاً بالبناء لا يدخل الدار من غير ذكر إلا إذا كان شيئاً جرى العرف فيه فيما بين الناس أن البائع لا يمنعه عن المشتري، فحينئذ يدخل وإن لم يذكره في البيع والمفتاح يدخل استحساناً ولا يدخل قياساً لأنه غير
Chapter of Sale, Section of Rights. (Matn:) Little and much that is in it or from it, and the upper floor enters with the purchase of a house like the privy, but not the terrace except with 'every right', and the road, watercourse, and water right do not enter except with something like 'every right', unlike lease. (Commentary:) The upper floor does not enter with the purchase of a residence unless the buyer utters one of the three terms because a residence resembles a house and a dwelling. In al-Kafi: This detail is based on Kufan custom; in our custom, the upper floor enters in all cases. In al-Dhakhira: Rights are mentioned for what is appurtenant to the sold item. Al-Masabih defines marafiq. The summary: marfiq has two pronunciations except for the marfiq of a house. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: rights include watercourse, road, etc.; marafiq according to Abu Yusuf are the usufructs of the house, and in Zahir al-Riwayah they are the same as rights. Then the commentary on the phrase 'And it enters with the purchase of a house' means the upper floor enters with the purchase of a house even if not mentioned, because the term 'house' linguistically includes the upper floor. The commentary on 'like the privy' means it enters with the purchase of a house, and similarly the well of water, trees in the courtyard, and inner garden. As for external items, if larger or equal, they do not enter without condition. In our time, these items enter by the contract without saying 'every right'. The principle from al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya: whatever is construction in the house or attached to it enters by way of appurtenance; what is not attached does not enter unless custom dictates. The key enters by istihsan, not by qiyas, because it is not attached.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the sale of a house (دار) and what appurtenances are included by default. The matn states that the upper floor (علو) enters like the privy (كنيف), but the terrace (ظلة) does not unless explicitly mentioned. The commentary begins by explaining that when buying a 'residence' (منزل), the upper floor only enters if the buyer says one of three terms: 'dar', 'bayt', or 'manzil' because a residence has resemblance to both a house and a dwelling. The ruling is refined by custom: in Kufa, the detail was as described, but in our custom (the commentator's era), the upper floor enters regardless of the term used. This establishes a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: 'ahkam are based on custom' (الأحكام تبتني على العرف). The passage then defines 'rights' (حقوق) and 'amenities' (مرافق), noting that rights are appurtenant to the sold item, and marafiq are the utilities. Differences between Abu Yusuf and Zahir al-Riwayah regarding marafiq are noted. The commentary then confirms that the upper floor enters with the sale of a 'dar' because linguistically a dar includes the upper floor. Similarly, the privy enters because it is part of the house. For external items, custom in the commentator's time leads to automatic inclusion without explicit mention. The discussion ends with the ruling on the key (مفتاح): by qiyas it should not enter because it is not attached to the building, but by istihsan based on custom it does enter. This illustrates the Hanafi methodology of giving precedence to custom (urf) over strict analogy when custom is established.

Key Terms
علو (uluww): The upper floor or loft of a house
كنيف (kanif): Privy or latrine, typically located within the house
دار (dar): A house or dwelling that includes a courtyard and is enclosed by boundaries
حقوق (huquq): Rights or appurtenances attached to the sold property, such as right of way and water rights
مرافق (marafiq): Amenities or utilities of a house, such as a kitchen, privy, or watercourse
عرف (urf): Customary practice recognized as a source of law

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, by which legal method does the key (miftah) enter the sale of a house?
A) By qiyas, because the key is attached to the house.
B) By istihsan, based on customary practice, overriding qiyas.
C) By explicit mention in the contract.
D) By default because the key is considered part of the dar.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, appurtenances like the upper floor and privy are included in the sale of a house (dar) by default, but custom (urf) can override both default and qiyas, as seen in the key example and the principle 'ahkam are based on custom'.

Exclusion of Appurtenances in Sale and the Role of Custom
Commentary explaining default exclusion of appurtenances in sale vs automatic inclusion in lease, plus istihsan for key.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on appurtenances (path, watercourse, water right) in a sale and contrast it with lease, and explain how istihsan based on custom includes the key.

Core Idea
Appurtenances are not included in sale unless stated; in lease they come automatically because usufruct depends on them.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a key principle in Hanafi sales law: certain rights and items that are attached to a property are not automatically transferred when the property is sold. However, lease contracts are different because their purpose—usufruct—requires these appurtenances. The commentary also introduces the role of custom (urf) and juristic preference (istihsan) in including items like the house key, even though strict analogy would exclude them. Understanding this distinction helps you avoid common contractual mistakes and appreciate how Hanafi fiqh balances textual rules with practical custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excluded items in a sale
List the four items mentioned: terrace (zullah), path (tariq), watercourse (masil), and water right (shurb). Note that in the matn only watercourse and water right are explicitly stated, but the commentary expands to include the path and terrace.
The terrace is disputed between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn if its entrance is inside the house; here the focus is on the other items.
2
Understand the reasoning for exclusion
These items are outside the boundaries of the sold property. Sale transfers ownership of the corpus (ayn), not necessarily the usufruct. Hence, they do not transfer unless explicitly mentioned (e.g., 'with all rights').
The phrase 'كل حق' (every right) is a typical expression to include appurtenances.
3
Contrast with lease (ijarah)
Lease transfers usufruct (manfa'ah). The lessee cannot benefit from the property without access to the path, watercourse, and water right. Therefore, these are automatically included in a lease contract.
Buying a young donkey or saline land is valid, but leasing them is not, showing the difference in purpose.
4
Analyze the key and ladder by custom
Strict analogy (qiyas) would exclude the key because it is not attached to the building. However, juristic preference (istihsan) based on custom includes it, since people normally hand over the key with the house. Similarly, an attached ladder is included.
The lock and its key remain excluded by qiyas.
5
Apply the rule to a path in another property
If the path or watercourse lies in a third party's property, it does not enter the sale unless explicitly mentioned, even if it is physically within the sold house's boundaries but owned by another.
Fakhr al-Islam's reasoning emphasizes that the buyer does not purchase the entire property, only a specific part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسيل والشرب إلا بنحو كل حق بخلاف الإجارة.
أي لا تدخل الثلاث في بيع الأرض أو المسكن إلا بذكر كل حق ونحوه بخلاف الإجارة حيث تدخل مطلقاً لأن كلاً منها خارج عن الحدود فكانت تابعة فتدخل بذكر التوابع، وإما الإجارة فإنما المقصود منها الانتفاع ولا يتحقق إلا بها ولأن البيع شرع لتمليك العين لا المنفعة بدليل صحة شراء جحش ومهر صغير وأرض سبخة ولا تصح إجارتها، وكذا لو استأجر علواً واستثنى الطريق فسدت بخلاف
متصل بالبناء فصار كثوب موضوع في الدار إلا أنا استحسنا وقلنا بالدخول بحكم العرف لأن العرف فيما بين الناس أن البائع للدار لا يمنع المفتاح عن المشتري ويسلمون الدار بتسليم المفتاح، والقفل ومفتاحه لا يدخلان والسلم إن كان متصلاً بالبناء يدخل، سواء كان من خشب أو مدر والسرر نظير السلالم. اهـ. قوله : (في بيع الأرض أو المسكن في القاموس : المسكن المنزل. وعبارة الهداية : ومن اشترى بيتاً في دار أو منزلاً أو مسكناً لم يكن له الطريق الخ. وكأنه أراد بالمسكن الدار. قوله: (وفي المعراج أراد الطريق الخاص الخ قال في فتح القدير وقال فخر الإسلام: وإذا كان طريق الدار المبيعة أو مسيل مائها في دار أخرى لا يدخل من غير ذكر الحقوق لأنه ليس من هذه الدار فلا تدخل إلا بذكر الحقوق إلا أن تعليله بقوله لأنه ليس من هذه الدار يقتضي أن الطريق الذي في هذه الدار يدخل وهو غير ما في الكتاب، فالحق أن كلاً منهما لا يدخل لأنه وإن كان في هذه الدار فلم يشتر هذه الدار إنما اشترى شيئاً معيناً منها فلا يدخل ملك البائع أو ملك الأجنبي إلا بذكره اهـ.
(Matn:) And the watercourse and water right except with something like 'every right', unlike lease. (Commentary:) Meaning the three (path, watercourse, water right) do not enter in the sale of land or residence except with mentioning 'every right' or similar, unlike lease where they enter absolutely because each is outside the boundaries; they are appurtenances and enter with mentioning appurtenances. As for lease, the purpose is usufruct and it is not realized without them, and because sale is legislated for transfer of ownership of the corpus, not usufruct, as evidenced by the validity of buying a young donkey, a small mahr, and saline land, while their lease is not valid. Similarly, if one leases an upper floor and excludes the path, the lease is invalid, unlike (sale). (Then continuation:) Attached to the building becomes like a garment placed in the house, except we have used istihsan and said it enters by custom because custom among people is that the seller of a house does not withhold the key from the buyer, and they deliver the house by delivering the key. The lock and its key do not enter. The ladder if attached to the building enters, whether of wood or mud, and the cradles are like ladders. Then: The commentator's statement 'in the sale of land or residence' – in al-Qamus, 'maskan' means residence. In al-Hidayah: Whoever buys a house in a dar, or a manzil, or a maskan, does not have the path, etc. It appears he meant by maskan, dar. Then: The statement from al-Mi'raj: He meant the specific path, etc. In Fath al-Qadir, Fakhr al-Islam said: If the path of the sold house or its watercourse is in another house, it does not enter without mentioning rights because it is not from this house, so it does not enter except with mentioning rights. However, his reasoning 'because it is not from this house' implies that the path which is in this house does enter, which is contrary to the book. The truth is that neither enters because even if it is in this house, he did not buy this house; he bought a specific part of it, so the property of the seller or a third party does not enter except with its mention.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, focusing on items that are excluded from the sale unless explicitly mentioned. The matn states that the watercourse (مسيل) and water right (شرب) are not included except with something like 'every right', and this is unlike lease (إجارة). The commentary explains that these three items (path, watercourse, water right) are outside the boundaries of the sold property; thus in a sale, they do not enter by default. However, they are considered appurtenances that can be included by mentioning the rights. In a lease, however, these are automatically included because the purpose of lease is usufruct, and without access to these, the usufruct cannot be obtained. The text then shifts to additional rulings: the key and ladder. By strict analogy (qiyas), the key should not be included because it is not attached to the building, but by istihsan based on custom, it is included. The ladder, if attached to the building, is included. The passage also addresses a nuance regarding a path that lies within another property: such a path does not enter the sale unless explicitly mentioned, even if it is physically within the sold house's boundaries but belongs to another. This clarifies that ownership is specific.

Key Terms
ظلة (zullah): A terrace or canopy attached to a house, often over the entrance
طريق (tariq): Path or right of way leading to the property
مسيل (masil): Watercourse or drainage channel
شرب (shurb): Water right or access to water
إجارة (ijarah): Lease or rental contract
مفتاح (miftah): Key to the house

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because lease is a weaker contract than sale.
B) Because the purpose of lease is usufruct, which cannot be realized without these appurtenances.
C) Because the lessee is required to pay extra for these rights.
D) Because these items are always included in any contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, appurtenances like path, watercourse, and water right are not included in a sale unless explicitly mentioned, but in a lease they are automatically included; custom may also bring in items like the key through istihsan.

Inclusion of Latrine and Canopy in the Sale of a House
Customary inclusion of latrine and disputed inclusion of canopy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for the inclusion of the latrine and canopy in the sale of a house according to the Hanafi school, and explain the basis of the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
The latrine is included by custom; the canopy is not included unless explicitly mentioned because it is built on the road.

When buying a house, which attached structures are included in the sale? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that the latrine is always included by custom, but the canopy (zulla) above the door is subject to dispute among the early Hanafi masters. Understanding this distinction clarifies the role of custom (urf) and the principle that structures on public property do not automatically transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two appurtenances
Name the kanif (latrine) and the zulla (canopy) and their default rulings given in the passage.
The latrine is included; the canopy is disputed.
2
Examine the reasoning for the latrine
Understand why the latrine is included: it is considered part of the house by custom (urf), even if built outside.
3
Analyze the dispute over the canopy
State Abu Hanifa's view (not included because built on the road) and the Sahibayn's view (included if entrance is inside the house).
The key difference is the location of the foundation or entrance.
4
Identify the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if a buyer purchases a house without explicit mention, the canopy may or may not be included depending on which view is followed.
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, but the Sahibayn's view is also valid.
5
Note the role of explicit clauses
The phrase 'with every right' or similar expressions (e.g., 'with its appurtenances') ensures the canopy is included regardless of the dispute.
Also, the main door is included when selling with appurtenances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعد من الدار عرفاً. والكنيف المستراح وفي المصباح الكنيف الساتر ويسمى الترس كنيفاً يستر صاحبه. وقيل للمرحاض كنيف لأنه يستر قاضي الحاجة والجمع كنف مثل نذير لأنه ونذر اهـ. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الكنيف خارجاً مبنياً على الظلة لأنه يعد منها عادة. قوله : (لا الظلة إلا بكل حق أي لا تدخل الظلة في بيع الدار إلا إذا قال بكل حق وهي الساباط الذي يكون أحد طرفيه على الدار والآخر على الدار الأخرى أو على أسطوانات في السكة ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي الصحاح : والظلة بالضم كهيئة الصفة وقرئ في ظلل على الأرائك متكئون ﴾ [يس: ٥٦] والظلة أيضاً أول سحابة تظل عن الظلة أبي زيد وعذاب يوم تحته قالوا : غيم سموم والمظلة بالكسر البيت الكبير من الشعر .اهـ. وفي المغرب : قول الفقهاء ظلة الدار يريدون السدة التي تكون فوق الباب وإنما لا تدخل عند أبي حنيفة لأنها مبنية على الطريق فأخذت حكمه. وعندهما إن كان مفتحها في الدار تدخل مطلقاً لأنها من توابعها كالكنيف. وليس مراد المصنف بقوله إلا بكل حق القصر على هذا بل إنما المراد به أو بنحوه بأن يقال بمرافقها أو بكل قليل وكثير هو فيه ؛ كذا في البناية. وفي الخانية : ويدخل الباب الأعظم فيما إذا باع بيتاً أو داراً بمرافقة لأن الباب الأعظم من مرافقها اهـ.
It is considered part of the house by custom. The kanif is the privy. In al-Misbah: the kanif is a screen, and a shield is called kanif because it screens its bearer. It was said that the toilet is called kanif because it screens the one relieving himself. The plural is kunaf like nadhir. This was absolute, so it includes the case where the kanif is built outside on the canopy because it is customarily considered part of it. His statement: (the canopy is not included except with every right) meaning the canopy is not included in the sale of the house unless he says 'with every right'. It is the vaulted passage (sabat) that has one end on the house and the other on another house or on columns in the alley, as in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Sihah: al-zullah (with damm) is like a porch, and it is recited 'in zulal on couches reclining' [Ya Sin:56]. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the inclusion of certain appurtenances in the sale of a house. The latrine (kanif) is included by custom because it is considered part of the house, even if built outside on the canopy. The canopy (zulla) is a different matter: it is not automatically included because it is built over the road, thus taking the ruling of the road. Abu Hanifa holds that it is not included unless explicitly mentioned, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is included if its entrance is inside the house, as it is an appurtenance like the latrine. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'with every right' is not limited to this; one may also say 'with its appurtenances' or similar. Additionally, the main door (bab a'zam) is included when selling with appurtenances because it is part of them. The passage highlights the importance of custom and the principle that things built on public property do not automatically transfer with the house.

Key Terms
كنيف (kanif): latrine or toilet; considered part of the house by custom even if built outside.
ظلة (zulla): canopy or portico built over the entrance of a house, often extending over the road or attached to another house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Inclusion of Appurtenances in Sale and Lease
Ruling with proof (talil) and istihsan for custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing sale from lease regarding automatic inclusion of the path, drainage, and water right, and justify the exception for attached items using istihsan and custom.

Core Idea
Appurtenances necessary for benefit are automatically included in lease but not in sale.

When buying or leasing property, it is crucial to know which accessory rights transfer automatically. The Hanafi school distinguishes between sale and lease based on their fundamental purposes: sale transfers ownership of the property itself, while lease transfers the right to benefit. This lesson unpacks the ruling for three key appurtenances and explains the role of custom and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rights
List the path (tariq), drainage course (masil), and water right (shurb). Recognize that these are appurtenances external to the property boundaries.
These rights are considered 'huquq' and not part of the physical property.
2
Contrast the default rule in sale vs. lease
State the rule: in sale, they are not included unless explicitly mentioned (e.g., 'with every right'); in lease, they are included absolutely without mention.
This is the core distinction.
3
Understand the proof (dalil)
Analyze the evidence: sale is intended for transfer of ownership of the property ('ayn), not benefit, as shown by the validity of buying a young donkey but invalidity of leasing it. Lease is for benefit, which cannot be realized without these rights.
This talil explains why the rules differ.
4
Examine the istihsan for custom
Note the exception: items physically attached to the building (e.g., a ladder) are included by custom. The custom of delivering the house with the key is cited, but the key itself is not included.
Custom ('urf) overrides the general rule in this case.
5
Apply to the key and ladder examples
Test your understanding: the lock and key are not included, but a connected ladder (wood or mud) is. Cradles follow the same rule as ladders.
This shows the boundary of the custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يدخل الطريق والمسيل والشرب إلا بنحو كل حق بخلاف الإجارة) أي لا تدخل الثلاث في بيع الأرض أو المسكن إلا بذكر كل حق ونحوه بخلاف الإجارة حيث تدخل مطلقاً لأن كلاً منها خارج عن الحدود فكانت تابعة فتدخل بذكر التوابع، وإما الإجارة فإنما المقصود منها الانتفاع ولا يتحقق إلا بها ولأن البيع شرع لتمليك العين لا المنفعة بدليل صحة شراء جحش ومهر صغير وأرض سبخة ولا تصح إجارتها، وكذا لو استأجر علواً واستثنى الطريق فسدت بخلاف متصل بالبناء فصار كثوب موضوع في الدار إلا أنا استحسنا وقلنا بالدخول بحكم العرف لأن العرف فيما بين الناس أن البائع للدار لا يمنع المفتاح عن المشتري ويسلمون الدار بتسليم المفتاح، والقفل ومفتاحه لا يدخلان والسلم إن كان متصلاً بالبناء يدخل، سواء كان من خشب أو مدر والسرر نظير السلالم. اهـ.
His statement: (The path, drainage course, and water right are not included except by something like 'every right', unlike lease) meaning these three are not included in the sale of land or dwelling except by mentioning 'every right' or similar; unlike lease where they are included absolutely because each of them is outside the boundaries so they are appurtenances, so they are included by mentioning appurtenances. As for lease, the purpose is benefit and it is not realized without them, and because sale was legislated for ownership of the property, not benefit, as evidenced by the validity of purchasing a young donkey, a small dowry, and barren land, but their lease is not valid. Similarly, if one leases an upper room and excludes the path, the lease is invalid; unlike what is connected to the building, for it becomes like a cloth placed in the house. Except that we applied istihsan and ruled for inclusion by custom, because the custom among people is that the seller of a house does not withhold the key from the buyer, and they deliver the house by delivering the key. The lock and its key are not included, but the ladder if connected to the building is included, whether of wood or mud, and cradles are like ladders.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage details the rights (huquq) that are not automatically included in a sale. The path (tariq), drainage course (masil), and water right (shurb) are physical rights external to the property boundaries; they are not part of the sold property itself. Therefore, in a sale, they are not included unless explicitly mentioned, e.g., 'with every right' or similar. In a lease (ijara), however, they are included by default because the purpose of lease is usufruct, and these rights are necessary for enjoyment. The text gives a proof: buying a young donkey, a small dowry, or barren land is valid, but leasing them is not (because you cannot lease something that cannot be used). This shows that sale and lease differ fundamentally. The commentator also notes that if something is physically connected to the building (like an attached ladder), it is included by istihsan due to custom. The custom of delivering the house with the key is invoked, though the lock and key themselves are not included. The ladder (sullam) if attached is included; cradles (surur) are analogous.

Key Terms
طريق (tariq): Path or right of way to a property.
مسيل (masil): Drainage course for water.
شرب (shurb): Water right or access to water.
سلم (sullam): Ladder; if attached to the building, it is included in the sale by custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, absolutely
B) No, they must be mentioned
C) Only if the tenant requests them
D) Only if the lease is for a long term

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, appurtenances like path, drainage, and water right are automatically included in lease but require explicit mention in sale; custom adds an exception for physically attached items.

Path and Drainage Rights Belonging to Another: Defect and Division
Passage ruling on when a path/drainage belonging to another is a defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a sold property has a path or drainage belonging to another, and identify whether it constitutes a defect giving the buyer the right of return.

Core Idea
A right of way for another is a defect, giving the buyer khiyar al-ayb.

This passage clarifies a crucial distinction: even if a path is physically located within a property, if it belongs to another, it is not transferred automatically and constitutes a defect. Understanding this helps avoid disputes in property sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine the type of property sold (house, land, etc.) and the nature of the right (path or drainage).
2
Determine inclusion in sale
Check whether the right is explicitly mentioned. If not, it is not transferred, especially if it belongs to a third party.
Distinguish between the path being in the sold property versus the sold property having a path in another.
3
Recognize defect
If the right belongs to someone other than the seller, it is a defect (ayb) giving the buyer the option to return.
4
Apply the fatwa case
Analyze the case of the father selling a vineyard with a path reserved and the subsequent sale to a stranger.
The reserved right is personal to the seller and does not bind later buyers unless stipulated.

His statement: (regarding the sale of land or dwelling) in al-Qamus: al-maskan is the house. The wording of al-Hidaya: 'Whoever buys a room in a house or a house or a dwelling, he does not have the path, etc.' It seems he meant by dwelling the house. His statement: (In al-Mi'raj, he meant the private path, etc.) al-Fath al-Qadir said: Fakhr al-Islam said: When the path of the sold house or the drainage of its water is in another house, it is not included without mentioning the rights because it is not from this house, so it is not included except by mentioning the rights. However, his reasoning 'because it is not from this house' implies that the path which is in this house is included, which is not in the book. The correct view is that neither is included because even if it is in this house, he did not buy this house but bought a specific part of it, so the property of the seller or a third party does not enter without mention. He sold the house with the drainage to someone with every right belonging to it, then sold the other house to someone else. The first buyer wanted to prevent the second buyer from draining water onto his roof. He said he can prevent him unless he stipulated at the time of sale that he did not sell the water drainage in the house he sold. End. I say: from this one knows the answer to the incident in the fatwa: A man had two vineyards, one having a path through the other. He sold the one containing the path to his daughter on condition that he could pass as before. Then she sold it to a third party. Can the father prevent the third party from passing? And what if he suffers harm from his passage? The answer: he cannot prevent him. Reflect on this. I saw the wording of al-Khulasa in my copy: he said he cannot prevent him. And I saw in al-Bazzaziyya that he does not have that, and it is attributed to al-Khulasa from al-Nawazil. I consulted al-Nawazil after it was problematic for me, and I saw he said he can prevent him, etc. Then I consulted al-Walwaljiyya and saw he said he has that. So I was certain it was a clerical error, and I corrected it in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya. Be vigilant, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage addresses several nuanced issues. First, if a sold house has a path or drainage that lies in another house (whether belonging to the seller or a third party), it is not included in the sale unless the rights are explicitly mentioned. Fakhr al-Islam and others clarify that even if the path is actually inside the sold house but belongs to another, it is not included. The correct view is that any property not physically part of the sold unit (even if within its boundaries) does not transfer unless specified. Second, if after the sale it is discovered that the house is burdened with a path or drainage for another property, and that other property belongs to a third party, this constitutes a defect (ayb) giving the buyer the right of return. However, if the other property belonged to the seller himself, the seller cannot exercise that right because he sold without reservation. Third, a fatwa from al-Ramli illustrates a case where a father sold a vineyard with a path to his daughter, retaining a right of passage; when the daughter sold to a stranger, the father sought to prevent the stranger from using the path. The answer is that he cannot prevent the stranger (according to one correct transmission), because the right was reserved only against the daughter, not subsequent buyers. This shows the importance of explicit stipulations. The commentator notes a textual discrepancy and corrects it.

Key Terms
عيب (ayb): A defect that gives the buyer the right to return the property (khiyar al-ayb). Here, the presence of a right of way for another is a defect.
مسيل (masil): Drainage course; if it belongs to another, it is a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After purchasing a house, it is discovered that there is a path in it that belongs to a third party's house. What is the ruling?
A) The sale is void (batil).
B) The buyer has the right to return the house due to defect (khiyar al-ayb).
C) The buyer must accept the house with the path.
D) The seller must provide another path.

Key Takeaway
A path or drainage belonging to a third party is a defect in the sold property, giving the buyer the right of return (khiyar al-ayb).

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Rights of Way and Water Drainage in Sale versus Division
Explaining inclusion of rights in sale vs division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sale and division regarding the inclusion of ancillary rights (path, water drainage) and state the Hanafi ruling for each transaction type.

Core Idea
Mentioning rights in sale includes the path; in division, it does not if an alternative exists.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a practical issue: when you buy a house, do you automatically get the right to walk across your neighbor's property to reach a public road? The answer depends on whether the transaction is a sale or a division of jointly owned property. The Hanafi school makes a fine distinction based on the nature of the contract and the availability of alternatives. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two transactions
Recognize that the passage contrasts sale (bay') with division (qisma) of jointly owned property. The rules for including ancillary rights differ between them.
Sale is a transfer of full ownership; division is a partition of existing shares.
2
Note the default: rights not automatically included
In both sale and division, rights such as a private path or water drainage are not automatically transferred unless explicitly mentioned. The default is exclusion.
This contrasts with the general principle that appurtenances are included unless excluded.
3
Distinguish sale from division
In a sale, if the seller mentions 'rights' (al-huquq) generically, the current path or water drainage is included. In a division, even if rights are mentioned, the path is not included if the co-owner can open another door or find an alternative access.
The key factor is the possibility of an alternative (imkan al-istighna).
4
Analyze the fatwa incident
The passage discusses a case: a father sells his garden with a right of passage to his daughter. She sells to a stranger. The father wants to continue using the path. The author, after reviewing conflicting sources, concludes that the father cannot be prevented from passing, because the path right was not explicitly excluded in the father's sale.
This shows the importance of explicit stipulation.
5
Understand the rationale (istihsan)
The difference between sale and division is based on istihsan (juristic preference). In sale, the buyer needs the rights for beneficial use, so mentioning rights is sufficient. In division, each co-sharer already has a right, and if an alternative exists, strict adherence to mention would cause harm (haraj), so the default is non-inclusion.
The underlying principle is avoiding unnecessary restriction on property use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Sale, Chapter of Rights. Sale is where one trades in an asset and sells it to another, achieving the desired benefit. In al-Mi'raj: He intended a private path on someone's property; as for a path to a dead end or to a public road, it is included. In al-Muhit: Likewise, what he has of the right to water drainage or throwing snow on someone's property out of need. In al-Dhakhira regarding rights: Only the path that exists at the time of sale is included, not the path that existed before the sale; so if someone blocks his house's path and makes another path and sells the house with its rights, the second path is included in the sale, not the first. So in al-Binaya. If he mentions rights and the seller says the sold house has no path in another house, then the buyer does not deserve the path without proof, but he may return it due to defect. Likewise, if there are beams of another house on the sold house, if the beams belong to the seller, the seller is ordered to remove them; if they belong to another, it is a defect. Likewise, if a path or water drainage for another house appears in the sold house: if that house belongs to the seller, the seller has no right to... in the sold house because he sold it without exception; if that house belongs to someone else, it is a defect. So in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan. In al-Khulasa: The path is included in pledge and endowed charity like lease. Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the chapter on rights (huquq) in sale. The author explains that rights such as a private path or water drainage are included in the sale of a house only if they are mentioned in the contract. However, there is a distinction between two types of paths: those existing at the time of sale (included if mentioned) and those that existed before but were later changed (the new path is included, not the old). If the seller claims there is no path for the sold house in another property, the buyer cannot claim it without proof but may return the house as defective. Similarly, if beams or water drainage belonging to another property intrude, it is a defect unless the seller can remove them. The text then discusses a fatwa incident: a man sold a garden to his daughter with a right of passage, but when she sold it to a stranger, the father wanted to continue using the path. The author, after reviewing sources, concludes that the father cannot be prevented from passing. The passage then contrasts sale and division (qisma): in division, rights are not included unless explicitly mentioned and even then only if no alternative exists, whereas in sale they are included by merely mentioning 'rights'. The reasoning is based on istihsan and the different objectives of sale and division.

Key Terms
الحقوق (al-huquq): Rights pertaining to a property such as right of way, water drainage, or placing beams.
الطريق (al-tariq): A pathway providing access to a property; may be a private road or a path to a public road.
مسيل الماء (masil al-ma'): A channel or direction for water drainage from a roof or property.
القسمة (al-qisma): Division of jointly owned property into distinct shares.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the inclusion of ancillary rights depends on the transaction type: sale includes them by mentioning rights, division excludes them if an alternative exists.

Istihqaq: Bayyinah vs Iqrar and Application to Division
Opening of chapter on Istihqaq with ruling on bayyinah vs iqrar and division example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish bayyinah from iqrar in terms of transitivity and apply the ruling to division of property involving appurtenances.

Core Idea
Bayyinah is transitive proof binding on third parties; iqrar binds only the confessor. In division, rights of way are not automatically included unless explicitly agreed.

This lesson opens the chapter on Istihqaq (claiming a right). We begin by defining the term and then encounter a foundational ruling: bayyinah (evidence) is a transitive proof that affects others beyond the parties to the case, while iqrar (confession) is only binding on the one who admits. The commentator illustrates this with a detailed example from the division of a house with a portico and water drain, explaining when such a division is valid or invalid. Understanding this distinction is crucial for knowing how rights are established and transferred in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define Istihqaq
Begin by understanding the literal and technical meaning of istihqaq as 'seeking a right' or 'being entitled'. The author cites al-Farabi's definition.
Istihqaq is the state of having a right that can be claimed through evidence.
2
Identify the Ruling: Bayyinah vs Iqrar
The passage states: 'Al-Bayyinah is a transitive proof, not iqrar.' Grasp the distinction: bayyinah becomes a proof only when it is public testimony (two witnesses), and it binds third parties; iqrar only binds the one who confesses.
Ta'lil (reasoning): Bayyinah is public and can be verified, so it affects all; iqrar is a personal admission.
3
Analyze the Division Example
Work through the example: two men own a house with a portico, door, and water drain. They divide without mentioning the road or drain. One gets the portico and courtyard but cannot open his door or drain water. The division is fasid (invalid) because the rights of way are not included and cannot be created.
The key condition: if the missing right cannot be created elsewhere, the division is invalid.
4
Differentiate from Sale and Ijarah
Compare the ruling with sale: in a sale, mentioning rights of way includes them even if the buyer could create them, because the purpose of sale is transfer of ownership. In division, the purpose is exclusive use, so including another's right harms the purpose.
In ijarah, rights are included as a means to benefit, not as part of the contract.
5
Apply the Ta'lil to the Layered Ruling
The commentator quotes later sources: if the lacking right can be created, the division is valid even without mentioning rights, and mentioning them does not automatically include them. Explicit consent is required for inclusion.
This refines the rule: the burden is on the parties to agree; silence does not imply consent for rights over another's share.

Source Text

( البينة حجة متعدية لا الإقرار لأن البينة لا تصير حجة إلا كانت بين رجلين وفيها صفة وفيها بيت وباب البيت في الصفة ومسيل ماء ظهر البيت على ظهر الصفة واقتسما فأصاب الصفة أحدهما وقطعة من الساحة ولم يذكروا
