الإجارة لأن في الإجارة موضع الشرب ليس مما تناولته الإجارة ولكن يتوسل به إلى الانتفاع بالمستأجر، والآجر إنما يستوجب الأجر إذا تمكن المستأجر من الانتفاع ففي إدخال الشرب توفير المنفعة عليهما، وأما هنا فموضع الطريق والمسيل داخل في القسمة و موجب القسمة اختصاص كل واحد منهما بما هو نصيبه، فلو أثبتنا لأحدهما حقاً في نصيب الآخر تضرر به الآخر إلا إذا ذكر الحقوق والمرافق لأنه دليل الرضا به ثم فرق بين البيع والقسمة حيث يدخل الطريق والمسيل في البيع إذا ذكر الحقوق وإن أمكنه أن يفتح الباب فيما ابتاع ويسيل ماؤه فيه وفي القسمة لا يدخل. والفرق أن المقصود من القسمة تميز أحد الملكين عن الآخر واختصاص كل واحد من الانتفاع بنصيبه على وجه لا شركة للآخر فيه فلا يصار إلى الانتفاع بنصيب صاحبه إلا عند التعذر والانتفاع بنصيب صاحبه لا يخل بمقصود البيع فلهذا افترقا اهـ. هذا والمفهوم من هذا الكلام أن في القسمة إذا لم تذكر الحقوق ولم يمكنه إحداث مثلها في نصيبه ولو واحداً منها فالقسمة فاسدة ولا تدخل الحقوق التي كانت إلا بذكرها، وإن أمكنه إحداث مثلها فلا تدخل وإن ذكرت والقسمة صحيحة وهذا موافق لما ذكره المؤلف هنا. قال في النهر : والمذكور في نظم ابن وهبان أنه إذا لم يمكنه فتح باب وقد علم ذلك وقت القسمة صحت، وإن لم يعلم فسدت وفي الفتح ولا يدخل الطريق والمسيل فيها إلا برضا صريح ولا يكفي فيه ذكر الحقوق والمرافق .اهـ. قلت : الذي في الفتح مثل ما نقلناه عن الكفاية، والذي نقله عنه في النهر ذكره في الفتح فيما إذا ذكر الحقوق وأمكنه إحداثها، ومعناه أن دليل الرضا وهو ذكر الحقوق والمرافق لا يكفي كما يكفي فيما إذا لم يمكنه الإحداث بل لا بد في دخولها من صريح رضا شريكه وهذا موافق لما مر فتدبر .
Chapter of Istihqaq, and it is seeking claim. In al-Misbah: so-and-so is istahaqqa, i.e., istawjaba; al-Farabi and a group said it. So the thing is mustahaqq (with fatha) as a passive participle, and from it: the sale came out as mustahaqq, etc. And he mentioned it after the rights (chapter) for the suitability between them in wording and meaning. His saying: (Al-Bayyinah is a transitive proof, not iqrar) because bayyinah does not become proof except... there were between two men a portico and a house, and the door of the house was in the portico and a water drain of the house was on the roof of the portico, and they divided, so one got the portico and a piece of the courtyard, and they did not mention a road or water drain, and the owner of the house could not open his door in what he got of the courtyard nor drain his water in it, so the division is invalid. And the road and water drain are not included without mentioning the rights and appurtenances as an attempt to permit division, like in ijarah, because in ijarah the place of drinking is not part of what the ijarah covers, but it is a means to benefit from the leased property, and the lessor only deserves the wage if the lessee can benefit, so including the drinking facility provides benefit to both. But here, the place of road and water drain are part of the division, and the effect of division is that each one is exclusive to his share, so if we establish a right for one in the other's share, the other is harmed, unless rights and appurtenances are mentioned because it indicates consent. Then he differentiated between sale and division: in sale, the road and water drain are included if the rights are mentioned, even if he could open a door and drain water in what he bought; but in division, they are not included. The difference is that the purpose of division is to distinguish one ownership from the other and each one's exclusive use of his share with no partnership of the other, so one cannot use the other's share except when impossible, whereas using the other's share does not contradict the purpose of sale, so they differ. etc. And the understanding from this discussion is that in division, if the rights are not mentioned and he cannot create similar ones in his share, even one of them, then the division is invalid and the existing rights are not included except by mentioning them; but if he can create similar ones, then they are not included even if mentioned, and the division is valid. This agrees with what the author mentioned here. He said in al-Nahr: It is mentioned in Nazm Ibn Wahban that if he cannot open a door and he knew that at the time of division, it is valid; if he did not know, it is invalid. And in al-Fath: The road and water drain are only included with explicit consent, and mentioning rights and appurtenances is not sufficient. I say: What is in al-Fath is like what we quoted from al-Kifayah, and what al-Nahr quoted from him is mentioned in al-Fath regarding when he mentioned the rights and could create them; the meaning is that the evidence of consent, i.e., mentioning rights and appurtenances, is not sufficient as it is sufficient when he cannot create them; rather, their inclusion requires explicit consent of his partner. This agrees with what has passed, so consider.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter of Istihqaq (claim of right). The commentator first defines istihqaq as seeking a right, citing al-Farabi. Then he moves to the key ruling: bayyinah (evidence) is a transitive proof, meaning it affects others beyond the parties to the case, while iqrar (confession) is only binding on the confessor himself. The reason given is that bayyinah does not become proof unless two men provide testimony about a property division scenario. The commentator then gives a detailed example from division (qisma) of a house with a portico, door, and water drain. If the division does not mention rights of way and drainage, and the owner cannot access his part, the division is invalid. The discussion compares sale and ijarah to show that in division, rights are not automatically included even if mentioned, unlike sale where mentioning them suffices. The ultimate rule: if the rights are not mentioned and cannot be created anew, division is invalid; if they can be created, the division is valid even without inclusion. The commentator then quotes al-Nahr and al-Fath, noting a nuance: explicit consent is needed for inclusion, not just mentioning rights.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): Seeking or claiming a right; the state of being entitled to something.
بينة (bayyinah): Clear evidence, typically testimony of two witnesses or a single witness with an oath, that is binding on third parties.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or admission; a statement by a person that acknowledges a right against himself, binding only on the confessor.
قسمة (qisma): Division of jointly owned property among co-owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of division (qisma) of a house with a portico and water drain, when is the division considered invalid?
A) When the parties do not mention the rights of way and the owner cannot open a door or drain water in his share.
B) When the parties mention the rights of way but the owner can still open a door elsewhere.
C) When the parties explicitly agree to include the rights.
D) When the property is sold rather than divided.

Key Takeaway
Bayyinah binds third parties, iqrar only the confessor; in division, essential rights are not implied and must be explicitly agreed upon to avoid invalidity.

The Scope of a Qadi's Judgment: Which Rulings Bind All People?
Explains that qadi's judgment binds everyone in freedom, marriage, lineage, wala', but not property, and debates waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of judgments that are binding on all people according to the Hanafi school, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding waqf judgments.

Core Idea
A qadi's judgment binds all people only in freedom, marriage, lineage, and wala'—not in ownership or waqf (disputed).

The qadi's authority is general, but not every judgment he issues affects everyone. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies which rulings have universal effect, and highlights a key difference among Hanafi scholars regarding waqf. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a subsequent claim can be heard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Universal Categories
From the passage, list the specific types of judgments that bind all people: emancipation (itq), marriage (nikah), lineage (nasab), and wala' (patronage by emancipation). Note that the author cites al-Khulasa and al-Sughra as authorities.
These are the only categories explicitly stated as 'qada' 'ala al-kafah'.
2
Contrast with Ownership Judgments
Judgments regarding simple ownership (al-mulk) only bind the defendant and those who derived title from him. They are not universal. Understand why: ownership is a relative right, not a status like freedom.
The passage explicitly says: 'wa amma al-qada bi al-milk fa-qada' 'ala al-mudda'a alayhi wa 'ala man talaqqa al-milk minhu'.
3
Analyze the Waqf Dispute
Some scholars (al-Halwani, al-Sughdi) analogize waqf to emancipation, arguing a waqf judgment is universal. Others (al-Sadr al-Shahid, al-Imadi) hold it is not universal. Identify the basis of each view.
The dispute is recorded in al-Khulasa; al-Sadr al-Shahid says there is no narration, but fatwa follows the non-universal view.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq inclines toward al-Imadi's position that waqf judgment is not universal, thus claims of ownership are heard. State the mufta bihi: waqf judgments are not binding on all people.
The author says 'wa sahhaha al-'Imadi fi al-Fusul anna al-qada bihi laysa qada' 'ala al-kafah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقضاء القاضي وله ولاية عامة فينفذ قضاؤه في حق الكافة والإقرار حجة بنفسه لا يتوقف على القضاء وللمقر ولاية على نفسه دون غيره فيقتصر عليه ؛ كذا ذكر الشارح. وظاهره أن معنى التعدي أنه يكون القضاء به قضاء على كافة الناس في كل شيء قضى به بالبينة وليس كذلك، وإنما يكون القضاء على الكافة في العتق قال في الخلاصة : القضاء بحرية العبد قضاء في حق الناس كافة ا.هـ. وفي الصغرى من دعوى النكاح في كتاب الدعوى : إذا قضى القاضي لإنسان بنكاح امرأة أو بنسب أو بولاء عتاقة ثم ادعاه الآخر لا تسمع؛ ذكره في آخر الباب الرابع والمائه من أدب القاضي ا هـ . وأما القضاء بالوقف ففي الخلاصة من القضاء والقضاء بوقفية موضع هل يكون قضاء على الناس كافة؟ اختلف المشايخ فيه. وفي كتاب الدعوى : أرض في يد رجل ادعى رجل أن هذه الدار وقف من جهة فلان على جهة معلومة وأنه متولي ذلك الوقف وذكر الشرائط وأثبت بالبينة وقضى القاضي بالوقفية ثم جاء رجل وادعى أن هذه الأرض ملكه وحقه تسمع بخلاف العبد إذا ادعى العتق على إنسان وقضى القاضي بالعتق ثم ادعى رجل أن هذا العبد ملكه لا تسمع لأن القضاء بالعتق قضاء على جميع الناس كافة بخلاف الوقف قال الصدر الشهيد : لم نر لهذا رواية ولكن سمعت أن فتوى السيد أبي شجاع على هذا وفي فوائد شمس الأئمة الحلواني وركن الإسلام علي السغدي أن الوقف كالعتق في عدم سماع الدعوى بعد قضاء القاضي بالوقفية لأن الوقف بعد ما صح بشرائطه لا يبطل إلا في مواضع مخصوصة وكذا في النوازل ا هـ. وصحح العمادي في الفصول أن القضاء به ليس قضاء على الكافة فتسمع فيه دعوى الملك فقد ظهر بهذا أن القضاء يكون على الكافة في الحرية والنكاح والنسب والولاء خاصة، وفي الوقف يقتصر على الأصح . وأما القضاء بالملك فقضاء على المدعى عليه وعلى من تلقى الملك منه؛
العاشر في دعوى الوقف وليس فيها تصحيح أصلاً بل مجرد حكاية أنه قضاء على الكافة عن الإمام الحلواني والسندي وعدمه عن الفقيه أبي الليث والصدر الشهيد قال : وفي الفواكه البدرية لمولانا بدر الدين بن الغرس أن القضاء بالوقف لا يكون قضاء كلياً حتى تسمع فيه دعوى ملك وقف آخر وهو الصحيح .اهـ. قلت وعبارة جامع الفصولين القضاء بالوقفية قيل يكون على الناس كافة وقيل لا .
By the qadi's judgment, and he has general authority, so his judgment is executed regarding all people. Confession is proof in itself, not dependent on judgment, and the confessor has authority over himself but not others, so it is confined to him. This is what the commentator mentioned. Its apparent meaning is that the meaning of 'transgression' is that judgment by it is a judgment upon all people in everything judged by evidence, but that is not the case. Rather, judgment upon all people is only regarding emancipation. He said in al-Khulasa: Judgment regarding the freedom of a slave is a judgment regarding all people. End quote. And in al-Sughra from the claim of marriage in the Book of Claims: If the qadi judges for a person regarding the marriage of a woman, or regarding lineage, or regarding wala' of emancipation, then another claims it, it is not heard. It is mentioned at the end of the hundred and fourth chapter from Adab al-Qadi. End quote. As for judgment regarding waqf, in al-Khulasa from al-Qada: Is judgment regarding the waqf status of a place a judgment upon all people? The scholars differed about it. And in the Book of Claims: A piece of land in the hand of a man; a man claimed that this house is a waqf from so-and-so for a known purpose, and that he is the administrator of that waqf, and he mentioned the conditions and proved by evidence, and the qadi judged the waqf status. Then a man came and claimed that this land is his property and right; it is heard, contrary to the case of a slave where if he claimed emancipation from a person and the qadi judged emancipation, then a man claimed that this slave is his property, it is not heard because judgment of emancipation is a judgment upon all people, contrary to waqf. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: We have no narration for this, but I heard that the fatwa of Sayyid Abi Shuja' is upon this. And in the Benefits of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and Rukn al-Islam Ali al-Sughdi, that waqf is like emancipation in not hearing a claim after the qadi's judgment of waqf status, because waqf, after it is sound with its conditions, is not invalidated except in specific places, and similarly in al-Nawazil. End quote. And al-Imadi in al-Fusul authenticated that judgment about it is not a judgment upon all people, so a claim of ownership is heard in it. So it has become clear that judgment is upon all people specifically in freedom, marriage, lineage, and wala', and regarding waqf it is restricted to the more correct view. As for judgment regarding ownership, it is a judgment upon the defendant and upon those who derived ownership from him. The tenth in the claim of waqf: There is no verification originally, rather merely a narration that it is a judgment upon all people from Imam al-Halwani and al-Sindi, and its absence from al-Faqih Abi al-Layth and al-Sadr al-Shahid. He said: And in al-Fawakih al-Badriyya for our master Badr al-Din ibn al-Ghars, that judgment regarding waqf is not a universal judgment such that a claim of ownership of another waqf is heard, and that is the sound position. End quote. I said: And the expression of Jami' al-Fusulayn: Judgment regarding waqf status: it is said to be upon all people, and it is said not.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage discusses the scope of a qadi's judgment in terms of its binding effect on all people (kafah). The commentator first clarifies a common misunderstanding: the general authority of a qadi does not mean that every judgment by evidence is binding on everyone. Rather, only certain types of judgments have universal effect. The text identifies emancipation (itq) as one such case, citing al-Khulasa: a judgment freeing a slave binds all people. Similarly, a judgment on marriage, lineage, or wala' (patronage by emancipation) is binding on all, so if someone later claims the same woman or child, their claim is not heard. The passage then presents a significant intra-Hanafi dispute regarding waqf. Some scholars like al-Halwani and al-Sughdi analogize waqf to emancipation, arguing that a judgment of waqf status is universal, preventing later claims of ownership. Others like al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Imadi hold that it is not universal, so a subsequent claim of ownership is admissible. The author inclines toward the latter, stating that the correct position is that waiver judgment is not general. Finally, a judgment of simple ownership (al-mulk) only binds the defendant and those who derived title from him, not all people. The passage ends by noting a numbered tenth issue on waqf claims, summarizing the difference of opinion.

Key Terms
قضاء على الكافة (qada' 'ala al-kafah): A judgment that binds all people, such that subsequent claims by anyone are not heard.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A claim of right or ownership, especially when a third party asserts a prior right to property in the buyer's hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following judgments binds all people (kafah) according to the Hanafi school?
A) Judgment on marriage (nikah)
B) Judgment on ownership of land
C) Judgment on debt between two parties
D) Judgment on waqf (according to all scholars)

Key Takeaway
Only judgments on freedom, marriage, lineage, and wala' bind all people; ownership judgments bind only the parties, and waqf judgments are subject to dispute, with the preferred view being non-universal.

The Scope of Emancipation Judgments: Original vs. Dated Freedom
Distinguishes original freedom and dated emancipation in judicial binding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between original freedom and emancipation judgments based on absolute vs. dated ownership, and state the temporal scope of each.

Core Idea
An emancipation judgment binds all people absolutely only if the ownership was absolute; dated ownership limits the judgment from the date.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a crucial nuance: not all judgments of freedom have the same universal effect. The commentator, drawing from al-Durar, distinguishes between original freedom (al-hurriyya al-asliyya) and emancipation ('itq), and further highlights that the effect of an emancipation judgment depends on whether the former owner's ownership was absolute or dated. This distinction protects the rights of third parties who may have acquired rights before the judgment. The commentary also extends the same principle to agency, showing that proving agency against a person binds all people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of freedom
Distinguish between original freedom (a person never enslaved) and emancipation (a judicial declaration of freedom after enslavement). The passage states that original freedom binds all absolutely, while emancipation does so only under certain conditions.
Original freedom is the default state; emancipation is an acquired status through a judgment.
2
Understand the condition for universal effect of emancipation
The commentator explains that if the emancipation was based on absolute ownership (milk mutlaq) – i.e., the master's ownership was not limited in time – then the judgment binds all people absolutely, just like original freedom.
Absolute ownership means the master owned the slave without any temporal restriction or condition.
3
Trace the consequence of dated ownership
If the ownership was dated (mu'arrakh), e.g., 'I owned him from five years ago,' then the emancipation judgment binds the public only from that date, not before. This prevents the judgment from invalidating rights that existed prior to the date.
The date limits the retrospective effect of the judgment.
4
Apply the principle to the agency example
The commentator quotes Mu'in al-Hukkam: if a person proves agency (wakala) against another, the judgment of agency binds all people. The rationale is that proving the cause (sabab) – the agency – against a person establishes the right against all, similar to the freedom case.
This extends the same logic from emancipation to agency: proving a cause that affects third parties binds them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (في الحرية والنكاح والنسب والولاء أراد بالحرية بالعتق لأنه هو الذي ذكره سابقاً، وسيأتي عن الدرر ذكر الحرية الأصلية وتقييد العتق بما إذا كان في ملك مطلق لا مؤرخ ليكون بمنزلة الحرية الأصلية في كونه قضاء على الكافة مطلقاً وإلا يكون قضاء على الكافة من وقت التاريخ. وزاد في الحواشي الحموية على ما هنا ما في معين الحكام لو أحضر رجلاً وادعى عليه حقاً لموكله وأقام البينة على أنه وكله في استيفاء حقوقه والخصومة قبلت ويقضي بالوكالة ويكون القضاء عليه قضاء على كافة الناس لأنه ادعى عليه حقاً بسبب الوكالة فكان إثبات السبب عليه إثباتاً على الكافة حتى لو أحضر
His saying: (in freedom, marriage, lineage, and wala') – he meant by freedom emancipation, because that is what he mentioned previously. And from al-Durar there will come mention of original freedom and the restriction of emancipation to when it is in absolute ownership, not dated, so that it becomes like original freedom in being a judgment upon all people absolutely; otherwise it is a judgment upon all people from the date. And he added in al-Hawashi al-Hamawiyya to what is here: what is in Mu'in al-Hukkam: If he brings a man and claims against him a right for his client and establishes evidence that he appointed him as agent for collecting his rights and litigation, it is accepted, and he judges regarding agency, and the judgment upon him is a judgment upon all people because he claimed a right against him due to agency, so proving the cause against him is a proof against all people, even if he brings
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the previous ruling about the universal effect of judgments. The commentator explains that the phrase 'freedom' in the matn refers specifically to emancipation ('itq), because that was the example given earlier. He then introduces an important distinction from al-Durar: original freedom (al-hurriyya al-asliyya) has universal effect absolutely. If emancipation is established by a judgment, but the ownership of the slave was absolute (i.e., the master's ownership was not dated or limited), then the judgment of freedom also has universal effect. However, if the ownership was dated (e.g., 'I owned you from five years ago'), then the judgment only binds people from that date, not before. This nuanced rule prevents a claimant from being bound by a judgment that predates his own right. The commentator then references an addition from al-Hawashi al-Hamawiyya, quoting Mu'in al-Hukkam: if a person brings someone and claims a right due to agency, proves the agency by evidence, and the qadi rules on the agency, that judgment is also binding on all people. The reason is that the claimant has established a cause (agency) against the defendant, which effectively proves a right against all people. The text is cut off, but the implication is that anyone claiming a contradictory right later would not be heard.

Key Terms
الحرية الأصلية (al-hurriyya al-asliyya): Original or innate freedom (a person never enslaved). A judgment of this type binds all people absolutely.
ملك مطلق (milk mutlaq): Absolute ownership without any temporal limitation or condition.
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation; the act of freeing a slave. In this passage, it refers to a judicial declaration of freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a judgment of emancipation (itq) bind all people (kafah) absolutely?
A) Only when the owner explicitly declares emancipation
B) When the ownership of the slave was absolute and not dated
C) In all cases of emancipation regardless of circumstances
D) Only when the judgment is based on evidence

Key Takeaway
The universal binding effect of an emancipation judgment depends on whether the ownership was absolute or dated; dated ownership limits the binding to the period of ownership.

When a Judgment Binds Non-Parties: Transitive Effect in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains when a qadi's judgment against one party extends to others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition for a judgment against a buyer to also bind the seller, and similarly for heirs, by analyzing the defendant's statement.

Core Idea
A judgment binds a non-party only if the losing party expressly attributes his right to that non-party.

In Hanafi procedural law, a qadi's judgment can extend to persons who were not present in court, but only under specific conditions. This lesson explores the key rule: if the defendant mentions that he derived his right from someone (e.g., buyer says 'I bought from X'), that someone becomes bound by the judgment. The same principle applies in inheritance and other contexts. Understanding this prevents wasteful re-litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
The passage states a fiqh ruling about the effect of a judgment. Determine the act (judgment against buyer/heir) and the consequence (binding seller/testator).
This is a procedural rule, not an act of worship.
2
Extract the decisive condition
The buyer becomes bound? No: the seller becomes bound if the buyer says 'I bought it from so-and-so.' If he only says 'it is my property,' the seller remains free to claim. The condition is the buyer's explicit attribution.
Attribution is key: mentioning the source of ownership.
3
Apply the same condition to inheritance
In the inheritance case, the defeated brother did not say 'I inherited it from my father,' so the other brother's claim is heard. Had he said it, the other brother would be bound.
Same test: explicit attribution to the testator.
4
Consider the reverse scenario: testator as plaintiff
If the testator was the plaintiff (claimant) and died, the defendant (stranger) cannot claim directly against the heir using the same boundary. The rule is not symmetric: the non-party is bound only when the losing party attributes to him, not when the winning party attributes.
Binding only flows from the losing side's attribution.
5
Generalize to dated ownership and marriage
Judgment on dated ownership binds all from that date, not before. Similarly, a marriage judgment binds from the proven date. This mirrors the same principle: the judgment's effect is limited to the time frame attributed in the proof.
Temporal attribution also determines the scope of binding.

Thus in al-Khulasa. And in it before it: If the buyer becomes the defendant, does the seller become a defendant such that his claim is not heard? If the buyer says in response to the plaintiff's claim: 'It is my property because I bought it from so-and-so,' meaning from the seller, then the seller becomes a defendant such that his claim regarding this boundary is not heard, and the buyer has recourse to him for the price. But if he says in response: 'It is my property' and does not add more, the seller does not become a defendant, so his claim regarding this boundary is heard. And inheritance is like purchase, and it is stated in al-Jami' al-Kabir. Its scenario: A house in the hand of a man claiming it is his; then another came and claimed it is his, inherited from his father, and brought evidence, and the qadi judged in his favor against him. Then the brother of the defeated party came and claimed that this house belonged to his father, died and left it as inheritance between the defeated brother and him. The claimant brother is granted half the house because the defeated brother did not say in his response: 'It is my property because I inherited it from my father'; therefore, the other brother did not become a defendant, so his claim is heard. Similarly, if the possessor, being the defeated brother, admitted that he inherited it from his father after denying and after establishing evidence, and if he admitted that it is his father's before establishing evidence, the brother's claim is not heard. End quote. And before it he mentioned: If the testator becomes a defendant regarding a boundary and then dies, and his heir claims that boundary: if he claims inheritance from this testator, no; if he claims absolutely, it is heard. And if it is the reverse, i.e., the testator was the plaintiff and the defendant was a stranger, then when the testator died, the defendant claimed this boundary absolutely against his heir, it is not heard. And he mentioned in it, citing al-Sughra regarding a claim of debt against one of the heirs, and the plaintiff admitted that the deceased left nothing; the judgment against him is a judgment against the deceased. End quote. The summary is that judgment against the buyer is judgment against the seller under the previous condition.

And in Fath al-Qadir: Judgment of istihqaq of the sold property from the buyer's hand is judgment upon all, and neither party's claim of ownership is heard. And upon the heir is judgment upon the testator with its condition, and upon the testator is judgment upon the heir with its condition, and upon one heir is judgment upon the remaining heirs with its condition. And Munla Khusraw mentioned: From the chapter of istihqaq, a judgment on original freedom is a judgment upon all people such that no one's claim of ownership is heard, and similarly emancipation and its branches. As for judgment on dated ownership, it is upon all people from the date, not before. That means if Zaid said to Bikr: 'You are my slave; I owned you since five years ago,' and Bikr said: 'I was the slave of Bashar, who owned me since six years ago and freed me,' and he proved it, Zaid's claim is repelled. Then if 'Amr said to Bikr: 'You are my slave; I owned you since seven years ago and you are my property now,' and he proved it, it is accepted, and the judgment of his freedom is annulled, and he is made property. 
Another and claimed a right against him, he is not required to re-prove agency. End quote. His saying: (As for judgment on dated ownership et cetera) – Sayyid Abu al-Su'ud said in Hashiyat Miskin: Our teacher derived from Munla Khusraw's words that judgment on marriage for one who claimed and established it is a judgment in the right of all people from the date, so a claim of the time is not heard as long as the judged marriage remains. Commentary
This passage addresses the transitive effect of a qadi's judgment on parties beyond the immediate litigants. The first scenario from al-Khulasa: if a buyer is sued for property and in his defense says 'I bought it from so-and-so', then the seller is bound by the judgment (his subsequent claim is not heard). If the buyer simply says 'it is my property', the seller is not bound. The same rule applies in inheritance: if a person claims inheritance from a deceased, and the possessor does not attribute it to that deceased, the judgment does not bind other heirs. However, if the possessor admits it is from the deceased, then other heirs are bound. The passage also discusses cases where the testator was a defendant or plaintiff, affecting the hearing of the heir's claim. A summary from Fath al-Qadir states that judgment of istihqaq on a sold property binds all, and no party can later claim ownership. The last part, attributed to Munla Khusraw and then commented by Sayyid Abu al-Su'ud, draws an analogy between dated ownership and dated marriage: a judgment on a dated matter binds all people from that date, not before. Therefore, a later claim based on an earlier date may be accepted and override the previous judgment. The text ends abruptly with a garbled fragment.

Key Terms
مقضى عليه (maqdi 'alayh): The party against whom a judgment is passed; a defendant who loses the case.
الملك المؤرخ (al-milk al-mu'rakh): Ownership that is dated, i.e., with a specific starting point in time, as opposed to absolute ownership.

B) Only if the buyer says in his defense 'this is my property'
C) Only if the buyer says in his defense 'I bought it from the seller'
D) The seller is never bound by a judgment against the buyer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: a judgment binds a non-party only if the defendant expressly attributes his right to that person.

When Does a Judgment Against the Buyer Also Bind the Seller?
Explaining when a judgment against the buyer is considered a judgment against the seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition that determines whether a judgment against the buyer extends to the seller in property claims.

Core Idea
A judgment only binds the seller if the buyer explicitly attributed his ownership to the seller in his defense.

In property disputes, the court may rule against a buyer, but does that automatically make the seller bound by the judgment? According to Hanafi fiqh, the answer depends on the buyer's wording during the lawsuit. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the critical distinction, using examples from purchase and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key scenario
A buyer purchases property from a seller. A third party successfully claims the property in court, and the buyer loses. The question: does this judgment also bind the seller?
2
Extract the buyer's possible responses
Look at two cases: (1) the buyer says 'It is mine because I bought it from so-and-so' (naming the seller). (2) the buyer merely says 'It is mine' without mentioning any source.
The exact wording matters for the legal outcome.
3
Determine the consequence for the seller
In case (1), the seller becomes adjudged against — he cannot later claim the property, and the buyer can recover the price from him. In case (2), the seller is not bound and may still claim the property.
The condition is the explicit attribution of title to the seller.
4
Apply the same logic to inheritance
The text gives an example with brothers and a father's inheritance. The principle is identical: if the heir who loses did not say 'I inherited from our father,' the other heir can still claim his share.
Inheritance is treated like purchase for this rule.
5
Summarize the underlying principle
A judgment binds only those whose title was directly litigated. If the buyer's defense does not explicitly implicate the seller's title, the seller retains the right to sue.
This protects the seller from being bound without having his day in court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذا في الخلاصة. وفيها قبله : المشتري إذا صار مقضياً عليه هل يصير البائع مقضياً عليه حتى لا تسمع إن قال المشتري في جواب دعوى المدعي ملكي لأني اشتريته من فلان يعني من البائع صار البائع مقضياً عليه حتى لا تسمع دعوى البائع هذا المحدود ويرجع المشتري عليه بالثمن، أما إذا قال في الجواب ملكي ولم يزد عليه لا يصير البائع مقضياً عليه حتى تسمع دعواه هذا المحدود والأرث كالشراء وهو منصوص في الجامع الكبير وصورتها : دار في يد رجل يدعي أنها له فجاء آخر وادعى أنها له ورثها من أبيه وأقام البينة وقضى القاضي له عليه بها ثم جاء أخو المقضى عليه وادعى أن هذه الدار كانت لأبيه مات وتركها ميراثاً له بين الأخ المقضى عليه وبينه يقضى للأخ المدعي بنصف الدار لأن الأخ المقضى عليه لم يقل في الجواب ملكي لأني ورثتها من أبي فلم يصر الأخ الآخر حينئذ مقضياً عليه فتسمع دعواه، وكذا لو أقر ذو اليد وهو الأخ المقضى عليه أنه ورثها من أبيه بعد ما أنكر وبعد إقامة البينة، ولو أقر أنه أبيه قبل إقامة البينة لا تسمع دعوى الأخ ا هـ . وذكر قبله المورث إذا صار مقضياً عليه في محدود فـمـات فـادعى وارثه ذلك المحدود؛ إن ادعى الإرث من هذا المورث لا ، وإن ادعى مطلقاً تسمع، وإن كان على القلب بأن كان المورث مدعياً والمقضى عليه أجنبياً فلما مات المورث ادعى المقضى عليه هذا المحدود مطلقاً على وارثه لا تسمع. وذكر فيها معزياً إلى الصغرى في دعوى الدين على إحدى الورثة وقد أقر المدعي أن الميت لم يترك شيئاً القضاء عليه قضاء على الميت ا.هـ. وحاصله أن القضاء على المشتري قضاء على البائع بالشرط السابق .
Thus in al-Khulasa. And in it before it: If the buyer becomes adjudged against, does the seller become adjudged against so that his claim is not heard? If the buyer says in response to the claimant's lawsuit: 'It is my property because I bought it from so-and-so,' meaning from the seller, then the seller becomes adjudged against so that the seller's claim to this boundary is not heard, and the buyer can recover the price from him. But if he says in his response: 'It is my property' and does not add more, the seller does not become adjudged against, so his claim to this boundary is heard. And inheritance is like purchase, and it is stated in al-Jami' al-Kabir. Its example: a house in the hand of a man who claims it is his. Then another comes and claims it is his, inherited from his father, and produces evidence, and the judge rules in his favor against him. Then the brother of the adjudged person comes and claims that this house was their father's, who died and left it as inheritance between the adjudged brother and him. Then judgment is given for the claimant brother for half the house, because the adjudged brother did not say in his response: 'It is my property because I inherited it from my father,' so the other brother did not become adjudged, thus his claim is heard. Similarly, if the possessor (the adjudged brother) acknowledges that he inherited it from his father after denying and after presenting evidence, [it is not heard]; and if he acknowledged from his father before presenting evidence, the brother's claim is not heard. And before it, it is mentioned about the testator: if he becomes adjudged in a boundary and then dies, and his heir claims that boundary: if he claims inheritance from this testator, it is not [heard]; if he claims absolutely, it is heard. And if the case is reversed such that the testator was the claimant and the adjudged was a stranger, then when the testator dies, the adjudged claims this boundary absolutely against his heir, it is not heard. And it is mentioned in it, attributed to al-Sughra, regarding a claim of debt against one of the heirs while the claimant acknowledges that the deceased left nothing: judgment against him is judgment against the deceased. The summary is that judgment against the buyer is judgment against the seller under the previous condition.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the scope of a judicial decision (qada) in cases of property claims. It opens with a ruling from al-Khulasa: when a buyer is adjudged against (i.e., the court rules that the property he purchased actually belongs to a third-party claimant), whether that judgment also binds the seller. The key condition is whether the buyer, in his defense, explicitly attributed his ownership to the seller. If he said, 'It is mine because I bought it from so-and-so (the seller),' then the seller is considered 'adjudged against' as well—meaning the seller cannot later bring a fresh claim to that property. The buyer can then recover the price from the seller. However, if the buyer simply said, 'It is mine' without mentioning the seller, the seller is not bound and can still claim the property. The text then states that the same rule applies to inheritance (al-irth ka al-shira') and cites al-Jami' al-Kabir. An example: a house in someone's possession is claimed by another who proves inheritance. After judgment, a third brother claims the same house as heir. Since the original adjudged brother did not specify inheritance in his defense, the third brother's claim is heard and he gets half. The passage also covers scenarios involving testators, heirs, and the effect of acknowledgment (iqrar). The underlying principle: a judgment is only binding on a person if he had the opportunity to litigate the specific title he relied upon. The final summary reemphasizes the buyer-seller rule.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): A judicial decision or judgment that establishes a right and binds the parties.
مقضى عليه (maqdi 'alayh): The person against whom judgment is given; the losing party in a lawsuit.
محدود (maddad): A specific boundary or piece of land; the subject of a property claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a property claim, when does a judgment against the buyer also bind the seller?
A) Always, because the seller is the source of the buyer's title.
B) Only if the buyer said 'It is mine because I bought it from so-and-so.'
C) Only if the seller appears in court.
D) Never, because the buyer and seller are separate parties.

Key Takeaway
A judgment against the buyer binds the seller only if the buyer explicitly attributed his ownership to the seller in his defense.

Binding Scope of Absolute vs. Dated Judgment
Distinguishing absolute vs. dated judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a judgment of absolute freedom and a judgment of dated ownership, and state the binding scope of each.

Core Idea
A judgment of absolute freedom binds all; a judgment based on dated ownership only binds from that date.

In Hanafi law, judicial decisions about ownership or freedom have different binding force depending on whether they are absolute or based on a specific date. This lesson, drawing from Fath al-Qadir and Mulla Khusraw, explains this distinction with a practical example from the chapter of sales (buyu).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides judgments into two types: absolute ownership (like original freedom) and dated ownership. Understand that absolute judgment is like original freedom—conclusive against all.
The text uses 'hurriyyah asliyyah' for original freedom and 'mulk mu'arrakh' for dated ownership.
2
Locate the key quotation from Qadi Khan
Qadi Khan states that manumission in absolute ownership is like original freedom and its judgment is upon all people. Manumission in dated ownership is binding only from the date, not before.
This is from Sharh al-Ziyadat, a Zahir al-Riwayah text.
3
Analyze the example with Zayd, Bakr, and 'Amr
Bakr is adjudged free based on claim of ownership from 6 years ago. Then 'Amr claims ownership from 7 years ago with proof. The judgment of freedom is overturned because the dated judgment only binds from its date, leaving earlier claims open.
The example illustrates the practical application.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
Consider how this rule applies to disputes over inherited property or multiple claimants. A judgment of absolute freedom or ownership cannot be challenged, but a dated judgment can be overturned by an earlier claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير: إن القضاء باستحقاق المبيع من يد المشتري قضاء على الكل ولا تسمع دعوى أحدهما أنه ملكه وعلى الوارث قضاء على المورث بشرطه وعلى المورث قضاء على الوارث بشرطه، وعلى أحد الورثة قضاء على الباقي بشرطه . وذكر منلا خسرو ومن باب الاستحقاق والحكم بالحرية الأصلية حكم على الكافة حتى لا تسمع دعوى الملك أحد، من وكذا العتق وفروعه. وأما الحكم في الملك المؤرخ فعلى الكافة من التاريخ لا قبله يعني إذا قال زيد لبكر إنك عبدي ملكتك منذ خمسة أعوام فقال بكر إني كنت عبد بشر ملكني منذ ستة أعوام فأعتقني فبرهن عليه اندفع دعوى زيد، ثم إذا قال عمرو لبكر إنك عبدي ملكتك منذ سبعة أعوام وأنت ملكي الآن فبرهن عليه تقبل ويفسخ الحكم بحريته ويجعل ملكاً لعمرو، ويدل عليه أن قاضيخان قال في أول البيوع في شرح الزيادات فصارت مسائل الباب على قسمين: أحدهما عتق في ملك مطلق وهو بمنزلة حرية الأصل والقضاء به قضاء على كافة الناس، والثاني القضاء بالعتق في الملك المؤرخ وهو قضاء على كافة الناس من وقت التاريخ ولا يكون قضاء قبله فليكن هذا على ذكر منك فإن الكتب المشهورة خالية عن الفائدة ا هـ.
And in Fath al-Qadir: The judgment that the sold property is claimed from the hand of the buyer is a judgment against all, and the claim of either one that it is his property is not heard. And against the heir, judgment is against the testator under its condition, and against the testator, judgment is against the heir under its condition, and against one of the heirs, judgment is against the rest under its condition. And Mulla Khusraw mentioned: From the chapter of claim and the judgment of original freedom, it is a judgment upon all, so that no one's claim of ownership is heard—similarly for manumission and its branches. As for the judgment regarding dated ownership, it is upon all from the date, not before. That is, if Zayd says to Bakr: 'You are my slave, I owned you since five years,' and Bakr says: 'I was a slave of Bishr who owned me since six years and then freed me,' and he proves it, Zayd's claim is repelled. Then if 'Amr says to Bakr: 'You are my slave, I owned you since seven years and you are my property now,' and he proves it, it is accepted, and the judgment of his freedom is overturned, and he becomes property of 'Amr. This is supported by Qadi Khan who said in the beginning of al-Buyu' in Sharh al-Ziyadat: Thus the issues of the chapter fall into two types: one is manumission in absolute ownership, which is like original freedom and the judgment upon it is judgment upon all people; the second is judgment of manumission in dated ownership, which is judgment upon all people from the time of the date, and it is not a judgment prior to it. So let this be on your mind, for the well-known books are devoid of this benefit.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage from Fath al-Qadir and Mulla Khusraw explains the binding effect of judicial decisions regarding ownership and freedom. It first reiterates that a judgment of entitlement (istihqaq) against the buyer binds all parties: neither the buyer nor the seller can later claim ownership. This is extended to heirs and testators under the same condition. The key new point is the distinction between a judgment of absolute freedom (like original free birth) and a judgment of dated freedom (based on ownership from a specific date). A judgment of absolute freedom is conclusive against everyone—no one can later claim that person as a slave. However, if the judgment was based on a claim of ownership from a particular date, it only binds others from that date onward. An example is given: if a person is adjudged free based on a claim of ownership six years prior, and then a third party comes with proof of ownership from seven years prior, the new claim is accepted, overturning the previous freedom judgment. This ruling is supported by Qadi Khan's classification in Sharh al-Ziyadat. The text emphasizes that this nuance is not found in many other books, so it should be noted carefully.

Key Terms
حرية أصلية (hurriyyah asliyyah): Original freedom; a person born free, with no history of slavery.
ملك مؤرخ (mulk mu'arrakh): Dated ownership; ownership claimed with a specific starting date.
قضاء على الكافة (qada' 'ala al-kaffah): Judgment binding on all people; it is conclusive against any future claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a judgment of manumission in dated ownership is binding:
A) From the date of the judgment onwards, but not before.
B) From the date mentioned in the claim (the historical date), but not before that date.
C) For all time, just like original freedom.
D) Only between the litigants, not against third parties.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a judgment based on absolute ownership is conclusive against all, while a judgment based on dated ownership only binds from that date and can be overturned by an earlier claim.

Judgment by Evidence or Acknowledgment? The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence vs. acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the preferred Hanafi view is to judge by acknowledgment over evidence, and identify the exception for recourse.

Core Idea
Evidence is for the denier, not the acknowledger — so acknowledgment takes precedence, unless harm would result.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a claimant brings both evidence (bayyinah) and acknowledgment (iqrar) from the defendant, which should the judge base his ruling on? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute, ultimately resolving that the default is acknowledgment, but with a critical exception when the litigant needs the judgment for recourse against a third party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proofs
The claimant has both bayyinah (evidence) and iqrar (acknowledgment). Which one establishes the right? Understand that both exist simultaneously.
2
State the default Hanafi position
Evidence is only accepted against a denier. Since the defendant has acknowledged, the need for evidence vanishes. The default should be judgment by acknowledgment.
This is based on the principle that al-bayyinatu 'ala al-munkir (evidence is upon the denier).
3
Note the ikhtilaf among later scholars
Some mashayikh argued for judgment by evidence, perhaps because evidence was presented first or considered more reliable. Identify this difference of opinion.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the founding imams did not explicitly address this scenario.
4
Apply the tarjih from Rashid al-Din
The first view (acknowledgment) is more apparent, but if the litigant needs recourse (e.g., buyer vs seller), the judge may base it on evidence to prevent harm. This harmonizes the two opinions.
The exception is based on need (hajah) to avoid harm (darar).
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if judgment is by acknowledgment, the buyer cannot recover from his seller; if by evidence, he can. The choice affects the buyer's ability to seek recourse.
This shows how a subtle difference in procedural ruling has real financial consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن فروع التعدي إذا قضى بها دون الإقرار مسألة . في الاستحقاق إذا استحق ذلك المبيع ببينة رجع المشتري على بائعه بالثمن وبالإقرار لا . ومن مسائل الاستحقاق ما في جامع الفصولين: لو استحق بالبينة فطلب ثمنه من بائعه فقال المبيع لي وشهدا بزور فقال المشتري أنا أشهد بذلك وأنهما شهدا بزور فللمشتري أن يرجع بثمنه على بائعه مع هذا الإقرار إذا المبيع لم يسلم له فلا يحل ثمنه للبائع ثم قال المرجوع عليه عند الاستحقاق لو أقر بالاستحقاق ومع برهن الراجع على الاستحقاق كان له أن يرجع على بائعه إذ الحكم وقع ببينة لا بإقرار لأنه محتاج إلى أن يثبت عليه الاستحقاق ليمكنه الرجوع على بائعه وفيه: لو برهن المدعي ثم أقر المدعى عليه بالملك يقضى له بإقرار لا ببينة إذا البينة إنما تقبل على المنكر لا على المقر وفيه اختلاف المشايخ، فقيل يقضى بالإقرار، وقيل بالبينة والأول أظهر وأقرب إلى الصواب ا هـ . قوله : ( وفيه اختلاف المشايخ الخ) ذكر في فتح القدير عن فتاوى رشيد الدين أنه مشى أولاً على القول الثاني وفي آخر الباب قال: والأول أظهر وأقرب إلى الصواب. ثم قال : وهذا يناقض ما ذكره أولاً إلا أن تخص تلك بعارض الحاجة إلى الرجوع فيتحصل أنه إذا ثبت الحق بهما ينبغي على ما جعله الأظهر أن يقضي بالإقرار وإن سبقته إقامة البينة غير أن القاضي يتمكن من اعتبار قضائه بالبينة فعند تحقق حاجة الخصم إلى ذلك ينبغي أن يعتبر قضاء بها ليندفع الضرر عنه بالرجوع .اهـ. ولخصه في النهر بقوله : وتحصل من هذا أن عند ثبوت الحق بهما يقضي بالإقرار على الأظهر إلا عند الحاجة فبالبينة، وسيذكر المؤلف عبارته بتمامها في التتمة آخر هذا الفصل .
And from the branches of transgression [i.e., when judgment is given without acknowledgment]: an issue. In claim: if the sold item is claimed with evidence, the buyer can recover the price from his seller, but with acknowledgment, not. And from the issues of claim in Jami' al-Fusulayn: if it is claimed with evidence and he demands its price from his seller, and [the seller] says: 'The sold item is mine, and the two witnesses testified falsely,' and the buyer says: 'I testify to that, and that they testified falsely,' then the buyer may recover the price from his seller despite this acknowledgment, because the sold item was not delivered to him, so its price is not permissible for the seller. Then he said: The one against whom recourse is sought in claim: if he acknowledges the claim, and the one seeking recourse proves the claim, he may recover from his seller, because the judgment was given by evidence, not by acknowledgment, since he needs to establish the claim against him in order to enable recourse against his seller. And in it: if the plaintiff proves [by evidence] and then the defendant acknowledges ownership, judgment is given by his acknowledgment, not by evidence, because evidence is only accepted against a denier, not an acknowledger. And in it there is a difference among the later scholars: some said judgment is by acknowledgment, others by evidence, and the first is more apparent and closer to correctness. Then his statement: (And in it difference of the later scholars, etc.) is mentioned in Fath al-Qadir from the Fatawa of Rashid al-Din: He first followed the second opinion, then at the end of the chapter he said: The first is more apparent and closer to correctness. Then he said: This contradicts what he mentioned first, unless it is specified with the case of need for recourse. So it is concluded that when the right is established by both, the judgment should be by acknowledgment as the more apparent, even if the presentation of evidence preceded it, except that the judge is able to consider his judgment by evidence. So when the need of the litigant for that is realized, it should be considered a judgment by it to remove harm from him by recourse. And al-Nahr summarized it: From this, when the right is established by both, judgment is by acknowledgment on the more apparent view, except when there is need, then by evidence. And the author will mention his full statement at the end of this chapter.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses branches (furu') of the rule that when a claim (istihqaq) is made, the buyer's recourse against the seller depends on whether the claim was proven by evidence (bayyinah) or acknowledgment (iqrar). The initial ruling: if the claim is established by evidence, the buyer recovers the price; if by acknowledgment, he does not. A nuanced case from Jami' al-Fusulayn involves the buyer's own acknowledgment and its effect. Then a key point: if the plaintiff first proves by evidence and then the defendant acknowledges, some later scholars (mashayikh) said judgment is by acknowledgment (since the defendant's admission makes evidence irrelevant), while others said by evidence. The first view is deemed more apparent. However, there is a further refinement from Fath al-Qadir quoting Rashid al-Din: when both are present, the preferred view is judgment by acknowledgment, unless the litigant needs the judgment to be by evidence for recourse against his seller. In that case, the judge may consider the evidence to avoid harm. This resolution harmonizes the two opinions. The passage emphasizes that this is an important subtlety in the chapter of istihqaq.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two witnesses, that proves a claim.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession by a party against his own interest, establishing a right for the claimant.
اختلاف المشايخ (ikhtilaf al-mashayikh): Difference of opinion among later Hanafi jurists (post-classical), as opposed to the founding imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (al-azhar) in the passage, when both evidence and acknowledgment are present, the judge should:
A) Give judgment by evidence, because it was presented first.
B) Give judgment by acknowledgment, because the defendant has admitted the claim.
C) Ignore both and require new proof.
D) Let the litigant choose which to rely on.

Key Takeaway
When both evidence and acknowledgment exist, the preferred Hanafi view is to judge by acknowledgment, unless the litigant needs the evidence-based judgment for recourse.

Two Exceptions: When Acknowledgment Binds Others (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Presenting two cases where a woman's or hire's acknowledgment binds another, according to Abu Hanifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two exceptions to the rule that acknowledgment is restricted to the acknowledger, and compare Abu Hanifa's position with that of the Sahibayn, including the practical difference.

Core Idea
A wife's or hire's acknowledgment of debt can prevent the husband from traveling or dissolve the hire, even though acknowledgment normally only binds the acknowledger.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: under what circumstances can a person's acknowledgment affect a third party? While the general rule is that acknowledgment is 'qasir 'ala al-muqir' (restricted to the acknowledger), Abu Hanifa identified two exceptions involving a wife and a hired worker. The Sahibayn disagreed, holding that the third party's rights are not compromised. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi methodology for balancing individual liability and third-party interests.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the principle that a person's acknowledgment (iqrar) only affects the acknowledger's own legal capacity (dhimma) and does not bind others.
The text quotes Abu Hanifa's own principle: 'a person's acknowledgment against another is not valid.'
2
Identify the two exceptions
Find the two cases: (1) a wife acknowledges a debt while her husband plans to travel with her – this prevents the husband from taking her; (2) a hired worker (ajir) acknowledges a debt – this dissolves the hire contract.
These cases seem to violate the general rule.
3
Explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Understand that the acknowledgment directly affects the acknowledger's own dhimma, and the harm to the third party is a necessary consequence. Additionally, the wife/worker can borrow to repay the debt, so the harm is not absolute.
The key: the acknowledgment is on the acknowledger's own right, not on the third party's.
4
Explain the Sahibayn's objection
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the hired worker is not believed against the employer (the hire remains) and the wife is not believed against the husband (the husband may travel). They argue that acknowledgment cannot bind a third party, and the harm can be avoided if the person borrows.
Their concern is protecting the third party's rights from unilateral declarations.
5
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Compare outcomes: Under Abu Hanifa, the wife may be detained (preventing travel) and the hire is dissolved. Under the Sahibayn, the husband can travel with his wife, and the hire continues until the worker actually fails to perform.
This difference affects the rights of the husband/employer vis-à-vis creditors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد على أن الإقرار قاصر على المقر مسألتان : الأولى إذا أراد الزوج أن يسافر بمرأته فأقرت بدين لإنسان فإنه يمنعها من السفر الثانية إذا أقر الآجر بدين يصح وتنفسخ الإجارة ولم يقتصر الإقرار على المقر والجواب أن هذا الإقرار وإن كان على الغير لكنه الإقرار لأنه صادف خالص حق المقر وهو الذمة ثم لزم منه إتلاف حق الغير بالضرورة ولأن المرأة والآجر يقدران على الإنشاء بالاستقراض وهذا قول أبي حنيفة. وعندهما لا يصدق المؤجر في حق المستأجر ولا تنتقض الإجارة ولا تصدق المرأة في حق الزوج حتى لا يكون للمقر له حبسها وملازمتها، ولا يبطل حق الزوج في نقلها كذا ذكره العتابي في شرح الزيادات، وذكر قبله أصلاً لأبي حنيفة فقال : أصل الباب أن إقرار الإنسان على غيره لا يصح
And it was objected that acknowledgment is restricted to the acknowledger with two issues: first, if the husband wants to travel with his wife and she acknowledges a debt to someone, then it prevents her from traveling. Second, if the hired worker (ajir) acknowledges a debt, it becomes valid and the hire contract is dissolved, and the acknowledgment is not restricted to the acknowledger. The answer is that this acknowledgment, although it is against another, is nevertheless acknowledgment because it affected the exclusive right of the acknowledger, namely the dhimma (legal capacity), and then it necessarily entailed the destruction of the right of another. And because the woman and the hired worker are able to contract a loan (by borrowing) [to fulfill the debt]. This is the view of Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the hired worker is not believed against the employer, the hire contract is not dissolved, the woman is not believed against the husband, so the creditor does not have the right to detain her or keep her, and the husband's right to transport her is not invalidated. Thus mentioned by al-Atabi in Sharh al-Ziyadat. And before it, he mentioned a principle from Abu Hanifa: he said: The basis of the chapter is that a person's acknowledgment against another is not valid.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage addresses two exceptions to the general rule that a person's acknowledgment (iqrar) affects only himself and cannot bind others. The objection raised was: if acknowledgment is limited to the acknowledger, why in two cases does it affect others? First case: a wife acknowledges a debt; this prevents her husband from traveling with her (because she may be detained for the debt). Second case: a hired worker (ajir) acknowledges a debt; this dissolves the hire contract (because the worker's liability affects his ability to perform). The answer (following Abu Hanifa) is that these acknowledgments, though harming others, directly affect the acknowledger's own legal capacity (dhimma), and the harm to others is a necessary consequence. Moreover, the woman and worker have the ability to borrow to pay off the debt, so the harm is not inevitable. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) disagree: they hold that the hired worker is not believed against the employer (so the hire remains), and the wife is not believed against the husband (so the husband can still travel with her). This is a clear intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The text concludes by quoting Abu Hanifa's principle that a person's acknowledgment against another is not valid, implying that these are exceptions due to the special nature of the situation.

Key Terms
إقرار قاصر على المقر (iqrar qasir 'ala al-muqir): Acknowledgment that is restricted to the acknowledger; it only affects the one who makes it, not others.
الآجر (al-ajir): Hired worker; a person who performs work for wages.
ذمة (dhimma): Legal capacity or responsibility; the entity that bears rights and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does a wife's acknowledgment of debt prevent her husband from traveling with her?
A) Because the husband is responsible for the wife's debts.
B) Because the acknowledgment directly affects the wife's dhimma and necessarily prevents her from being moved until the debt is paid.
C) Because the creditor has a right to detain the husband.
D) Because the wife's acknowledgment overrides the husband's authority.

Key Takeaway
The two exceptions show that under certain conditions, a person's acknowledgment can affect third parties when it necessarily impacts their own legal capacity.

Precedence of Iqrar over Bayyina: Resolving the Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Resolves a Hanafi ikhtilaf on judging with proof vs. confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling for a judge when both iqrar and bayyina are present and explain the exception that permits using bayyina when recourse is needed.

Core Idea
Iqrar is stronger than bayyina unless the litigant needs bayyina to preserve recourse.

When both a confession and evidence are before a judge, which should he rely on? Early Hanafi scholars differed, some favoring the conclusive nature of confession, others the external proof of witnesses. Later authorities reconciled these views by establishing a default ruling with a specific exception. This lesson unpacks that resolution, showing how the judge's decision can enable a litigant's right to recourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ikhtilaf
Recognize the two opinions among Hanafi scholars: one says the judge should always rule by iqrar (confession), the other by bayyina (evidence).
2
Examine the apparent contradiction in Rashid al-Din's statements
Notice that Rashid al-Din initially followed the second opinion (iqrar) but later declared the first (bayyina) as more apparent. This contradiction is resolved by the need for recourse.
3
Extract the resolution: the conditional rule
Understand that the default is to rule by iqrar, but if the litigant needs the bayyina to enable recourse against a third party (e.g., to sue a seller), the judge may rule by bayyina instead.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a buyer who confesses but also has witnesses can preserve his right to sue the seller only if the judge rules by bayyina when necessary.
5
Note the source authority
The ruling is based on Fatawa Rashid al-Din and is summarized in al-Nahr, representing the mufta bihi position among later Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفيه اختلاف المشايخ الخ) ذكر في فتح القدير عن فتاوى رشيد الدين أنه مشى أولاً على القول الثاني وفي آخر الباب قال: والأول أظهر وأقرب إلى الصواب. ثم قال : وهذا يناقض ما ذكره أولاً إلا أن تخص تلك بعارض الحاجة إلى الرجوع فيتحصل أنه إذا ثبت الحق بهما ينبغي على ما جعله الأظهر أن يقضي بالإقرار وإن سبقته إقامة البينة غير أن القاضي يتمكن من اعتبار قضائه بالبينة فعند تحقق حاجة الخصم إلى ذلك ينبغي أن يعتبر قضاء بها ليندفع الضرر عنه بالرجوع .اهـ. ولخصه في النهر بقوله : وتحصل من هذا أن عند ثبوت الحق بهما يقضي بالإقرار على الأظهر إلا عند الحاجة فبالبينة، وسيذكر المؤلف عبارته بتمامها في التتمة آخر هذا الفصل .
He said: (And in it there is a difference of the scholars...) Mentioned in Fath al-Qadir from the Fatawa of Rashid al-Din that he initially followed the second opinion, and at the end of the chapter he said: The first is more apparent and closer to correctness. Then he said: This contradicts what he mentioned first unless that is specified by the circumstance of the need for recourse. It results from this that when the right is established by both, according to what he made the more apparent, the judge should rule by the confession even if the evidence was presented earlier. However, the judge is able to consider his ruling by evidence. When the need of the litigant for that [recourse] is realized, it is appropriate that he consider the ruling by evidence to repel harm from him by recourse. End. And it was summarized in al-Nahr by his statement: It results from this that when the right is established by both, he rules by confession on the more apparent view, except when there is a need, then by evidence. And the author will mention his statement in full at the end of this chapter.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars regarding which proof—bayyina (evidence) or iqrar (confession)—the judge should base his ruling on when both are present. The author, al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah, had mentioned a difference: some said judgment by iqrar, others by bayyina. Here, the commentator draws on Fath al-Qadir, which quotes Fatawa Rashid al-Din. Initially, Rashid al-Din followed the second opinion (iqrar takes precedence), but at the end of the chapter he declared the first opinion (bayyina) as more apparent, which seems contradictory. The resolution is that the general rule is to judge by iqrar because it is more conclusive (the person admits against himself), but if the litigant needs to use the bayyina for recourse against another (e.g., to sue the seller), then the judge may rule by bayyina to preserve the litigant's right. This principle is summarized in al-Nahr: the default is iqrar, except when there is a need for bayyina. The author promises to quote this in full at the end of the chapter.

Key Terms
اختلاف المشايخ (ikhtilaf al-mashayikh): A difference of opinion among later Hanafi authorities (after the founding imams).
بينة (bayyina): Evidence presented by a claimant to prove a right, typically two male witnesses or equivalent.
حاجة الرجوع (hajat al-ruju'): The need of the litigant to have the ruling based on evidence so that he can recourse against a third party (e.g., the seller).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When both bayyina and iqrar are present, what is the default ruling for a judge according to the reconciled view?
A) Always rule by bayyina.
B) Always rule by iqrar.
C) Rule by iqrar, but use bayyina if the litigant needs recourse.
D) Rule by neither, the case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, iqrar takes precedence over bayyina, but bayyina may be used when needed to enable recourse against a third party.
